وأما عند المعتزلة فأربعة أقسام أي : الصفة الثبوتية تنقسم عندهم إلى أقسام أربعة : انتفائها مع الذات فلا قوله : ( بناء على الحال) فإنه صفة قائمة بموجود فتكون دالة على معنى زائد على الذات كونه صفة نفسية بذلك المعنى مع كون بعض أفراده منها كالجوهرية واللونية والسوادية. قوله: (ما لا يصح توهم إلخ) أي لا يكون توهم الارتفاع صحيحاً مطابقاً للواقع ولذا لم يقل ما لا يتوهم فإن التوهم ممكن بل واقع لكن خلاف ما في نفس الأمر. قوله : ( ولا يمكن تصور انتفائها إلخ) أي تصوراً مطابقاً للواقع فلا ينافي ما تقرر من يمكن تصور انفكاك اللازم عن الملزوم وإن كان المتصور محالاً بخلاف الذاتي فإنَّ التصور فيه كالمتصور محال . أنه قوله : ( إلى أقسام أربعة) بتقسيمين الأول الصفة الثبوتية إما أن تكون أخص صفات النفس فهي الصفة النفسية أو لا، وهي إما أن تكون معللة بمعنى زائد على الذات، فهي المعللة أو لا تكون معللة؛ كالعلم والقدرة منا والعالمية والقادرية للواجب تعالى فعلى هذا يتحقق الواسطة النفسية والمعنوية. والثاني الصفة إما أن تكون حاصلة بتأثير الفاعل وهي الحدوث أو تابعة لها من غير تأثير متجدد فيها سواء كانت معللة بمعنى زائد أو لا، والصفات النفسية خارجة عن القسمين. قوله : ( بناء على الحال ) وكونها زائدة على الذات مع كونها من صفات النفس كما مر . قوله : ( ما لا يصح توهم ارتفاعه عن الذات قد سبق توجيهه في المقصد التاسع من مرصد الوحدة والكثرة فلينظر فيه . الموقف الثالث - المقدمة ( الأول ) الصفة النفسية فقال الجبائي وأتباعه منهم وهي أخص وصف النفس وهي التي بها يقع التماثل بين المتماثلين والتخالف بين المتخالفين كالسوادية والبياضية ولم يجوزوا اجتماع صفتي النفس في ذات واحدة ولم يجعلوا اللونية مثلاً صفة نفسية للسواد والبياض وقال الأكثرون من المعتزلة الصفة النفسية هي الصفة اللازمة للذات فجوزوه أي جوزوا بناء على ذلك اجتماع صفتي نفس في ذات واحدة لأن الصفات اللازمة لشيء واحد متعددة ككون السواد سواداً ولوناً وشيئاً وعرضاً ويدخل في ذلك كون الرب تعالى عالماً وقادراً فإنه لازم لذاته واتفقوا وفي نسخة المصنف وأثبتوا أنها أي الصفة النفسية يشترك فيها الموجود والمعدوم قوله : ( وهي التي إلخ ) زاد الشارح قدس سره لفظ، وهي إشارة إلى أن الموصول مع الصلة خبر، لقوله هي بيان لحكم الصفة النفسية لزيادة التوضيح ؛ وليست صفة تقييدية لإخراج شيء، فإن أخص وصف الشيء لا يكون إلا مأخوذاً من تمام الماهية؛ بخلاف المأخوذ من الجنس، فإنه أعم منه صدقاً؛ والمأخوذ من الفصل القريب فإنه أعم مفهوماً، وإن كان مساوياً له من الصدق كالناطقية والإنسانية . قوله : ( ولم يجوزوا إلخ ) الامتناع أن يكون لشيء واحد ماهيتان قوله : ( ولم يجعلوا إلخ وكذا القابضية والأولى التعرض لها قوله : (الصفة اللازمة ) وعلى هذا لا واسطة بين النفسية والمعنوية . قوله : (أخص وصف النفس إلخ ) قد بينا فيما سبق أن المراد وصف لا أخص منه لا أنها أخص من جميع أوصاف النفس لتحقق الصفة النفسية في المركبات التي فصلها يساوي نوعها لكن التماثل بالنوع فيخرج الفصل بقوله : التي بها يقع التماثل وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يجعل وصف النفس أعم من الصفة النفسية حتى لا ينافي قولهم بعدم جواز اجتماع صفتي النفس ثم إن قادرية الله تعالى وعالميته خارجة عن الأقسام الأربعة على تقسيم الجبائي إلا أن يدرجها في الصفة المعنوية، ويقول بتعليلها بالألوهية كما يقول به ابنه أبو هاشم ثم الأجناس والفصول وكذا لوازم الماهية أيضاً خارجة على تقسيمه من الأربعة ولا يجدي أن يقال: مذهبه أن الكل مشترك في الذات والحقيقة والتمايز بالأحوال فقط على ما سيجيء من الإلهيات لأن الكلام في الحيوان والناطق سواء عدا جنساً وفصلاً أم لا إلا أن يدرجها في المعنوية ويقول بتعليل الحيوانية والناطقية بالإنسانية ويجعل الإنسانية صفة نفسية . قوله : ( يشترك فيها الموجود والمعدوم فإن قلت : العالمية والقادرية ونحوهما من توابع فلا الحياة عندهم يوصف بهما المعدوم مع أنهم عدوها من الأحوال فكيف يصح الحكم بوجود اشتراك المعدوم والموجود في الصفات النفسية قلت هم يجوزون اتصاف المعدوم بالصفات المذكورة ولا ينافيه عدهم إياها من توابع الحياة لأن المعدوم وعندهم متصف بالحياة ولذا عده الرازي جهالة بينة كما سبق في خاتمة المقصد السادس في أن المعدوم شيء أم لا .
And as for the Mu'tazilites, there are four divisions, i.e., the positive attribute (al-sifa al-thubutiyya) is divided among them into four divisions: its negation with the essence? So no. He said: (based on the hal) for it is an attribute subsisting in an existent, so it indicates an additional meaning upon the essence, being a psychological attribute in that sense, with some of its individuals being like substantiality, colority, and blackness. He said: (That of which it is not valid to imagine removal etc.) meaning that the imagination of removal is not correct matching reality, so he did not say 'that which is not imagined' because imagination is possible, indeed actual, but contrary to what is in actual fact. He said: (And it is not possible to imagine its negation etc.) meaning an imagination matching reality, so it does not contradict what is established that it is possible to imagine the separation of the necessary from the necessitated even if the imagined is impossible, unlike the essential, for the imagination of it is like the imagined impossible. That he said: (into four divisions) by two divisions: first, the positive attribute is either the most specific description of the essence, and it is the psychological attribute, or not, and it is either caused by an additional meaning upon the essence, so it is the caused attribute, or not caused, like knowledge and power from us and being knowing and being able for the exalted Necessary, so on this basis the intermediate of the psychological and rational is realized. Second, the attribute is either produced by the agent's effect and it is contingency (al-huduth), or following it without renewed effect in it, whether it is caused by an additional meaning or not, and the psychological attributes are outside the two divisions. He said: (based on the hal) and its being additional upon the essence while being from the attributes of the essence as passed. He said: (That of which it is not valid to imagine removal from the essence) its guidance has preceded in the ninth section of the observation of unity and multiplicity, so let it be looked at. The third position - introduction: (The first) the psychological attribute. Al-Jubba'i and his followers among them said: it is the most specific description of the essence, and it is that by which similarity occurs between similar things and difference between different things, like blackness and whiteness. And they did not allow two psychological attributes to combine in one essence, and they did not make colority, for example, a psychological attribute of black and white. And the majority of the Mu'tazilites said: the psychological attribute is the necessary attribute for the essence, so they allowed that, i.e., they allowed based on that the combination of two psychological attributes in one essence because the necessary attributes for one thing are multiple, like black being black, color, thing, accident, and this includes the Lord's being knowing and able, for it is necessary for His essence. And they agreed - and in the copy of the author: 'and they affirmed' - that it, i.e., the psychological attribute, is shared between the existent and the non-existent. He said: (and it is that which etc.) The commentator, may his secret be sanctified, added the word 'and it', indicating that the relative clause is the predicate of 'it', clarifying the ruling of the psychological attribute for further elucidation, and it is not a restrictive attribute to exclude something, for the most specific description of a thing is only taken from the entirety of the quiddity, unlike that taken from the genus, for it is more general than it in extension, and that taken from the proximate differentia, for it is more general in intension, even if it is equal to it in extension, like rationality and humanity. He said: (And they did not allow etc.) the impossibility that one thing has two quiddities. He said: (And they did not make etc.) and likewise grasping? and it is better to mention it. He said: (the necessary attribute) and on this there is no intermediate between the psychological and the rational. He said: (The most specific description of the essence etc.) we have clarified previously that the meaning is a description than which there is no more specific, not that it is more specific than all descriptions of the essence, because the psychological attribute is realized in composites whose specific difference equals their species, but similarity by species, so the differentia is excluded by his saying 'that by which similarity occurs'. And on this it is fitting to make 'description of the essence' more general than 'psychological attribute' so as not to contradict their view of the impermissibility of combining two psychological attributes. Then indeed the being-able of God and His being-knowing are outside the four divisions according to al-Jubba'i's division, unless he includes them in the rational attribute and says they are caused by divinity, as his son Abu Hashim says. Then the genera and differentiae and likewise the concomitants of the quiddity are also outside his four divisions, and it does not avail to say: his doctrine is that all share in the essence and reality and differentiation is only by states, as will come in theology, because the discussion is about animal and rational, whether they are genus and differentia or not, unless he includes them in the rational and says the animality and rationality are caused by humanity and makes humanity a psychological attribute. He said: (the existent and non-existent share in it) For if you say: being-knowing and being-able and similar are among the consequences of life, so the non-existent is not described by them according to them, even though they counted them as states, so how is the judgment of the sharing of the non-existent and the existent in the psychological attributes valid? I say: they allow the non-existent to be described by the mentioned attributes, and their counting them as consequences of life does not contradict that, because the non-existent in their view is described by life, and for this reason al-Razi considered it manifest ignorance, as previously in the conclusion of the sixth section on whether the non-existent is a thing or not.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazilite classification of positive attributes (al-sifat al-thubutiyya) into four categories. The author first states that the Mu'tazilites divide positive attributes into four divisions. The commentary clarifies that there are two ways to divide them. First, by whether the attribute is the most specific description of the essence (which is the psychological attribute) or not. If not, it is either caused by an additional meaning (mu'allala) or not caused (like knowledge and power in us, and being-knowing and being-able in God). Second, by whether the attribute is produced by the agent's effect (contingency) or follows without renewed effect. Psychological attributes are outside both of these divisions. The passage then presents the first category: the psychological attribute (al-nafsiyya). Two views are reported: al-Jubba'i and his followers define it as the most specific description of the essence, causing similarity and difference (e.g., blackness and whiteness), and they do not allow multiple such attributes in one essence nor consider colority a psychological attribute. The majority of Mu'tazilites define it as the necessary attribute (lazima) for the essence, allowing multiple such attributes (e.g., black is both black and a color). They also agree that psychological attributes apply to both existent and non-existent. The commentary then elaborates on the wording, explaining that the added phrase 'wa-hiya' indicates the predicate. It clarifies that the 'most specific description' must be from the complete quiddity, not genus or differentia. It discusses the impossibility of two quiddities for one thing and the issue of God's attributes: al-Jubba'i's four divisions exclude God's being-knowing and being-able unless included as rational attributes caused by divinity. The passage ends by defending the claim that psychological attributes apply to non-existents, despite the objection that being-knowing and being-able depend on life; the Mu'tazilites allow the non-existent to be described with life, hence also with these attributes.

الصفة اللازمة
الجبائي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that psychological attributes only apply to existent beings.
Correction: The passage states that the majority of Mu'tazilites affirm that psychological attributes are shared between existent and non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, why can two psychological attributes not combine in one essence?
A) Because an essence cannot have two necessary attributes.
B) Because each essence has only one most specific description.
C) Because the majority prohibits it.
D) Because psychological attributes are only for non-existents.

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'psychological attribute' is contested: al-Jubba'i restricts it to the most specific description, while the majority expands it to any necessary attribute, and both apply it to non-existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate God's attributes like knowledge and power, do you truly believe they are essential to His being, or do you unconsciously imagine them as separate additions? How would your prayer change if you were certain that every attribute is nothing but His very essence?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted that psychological attributes apply even to non-existents—things that do not yet exist. Do you treat God's knowledge of your future sins and deeds as real and present before you commit them? How does that awareness shape the choices you make today?
?
Al-Jubba'i defined the psychological attribute as 'the most specific description'—a limit beyond which nothing more can be said. Is there an area of your life where you have allowed a secondary description (e.g., 'I am a sinner' or 'I am weak') to define you more than your essential identity as a servant of God? Will you reclaim that territory today?

The Definition of Substance and Its Non-Applicability to Allah
The author states the theologians' view that existence is additional to essence in substances and accidents, so the definition of substance does not apply to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theologians hold that the definition of substance does not apply to Allah because existence is additional to essence.

Core Idea
The definition of substance implies existence is added to essence, so it cannot describe Allah.

The theologians define substance as a quiddity that, when it exists, is not in a subject. They intentionally include the phrase 'when it exists' to signal that existence is conceptually distinct from the essence of the substance. This distinction becomes crucial when considering the Divine Essence, for in Allah existence is precisely the same as His essence, making the definition inapplicable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the initial claim that the definition of substance does not apply to Allah.
Notice the phrase 'when it exists' in the definition.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the definition of substance (a quiddity that when it exists is not in a subject) and the condition 'when it exists'.
The condition implies existence is additional.
3
Understand the implication
Realize that the condition implies existence is additional to essence, which is not true for Allah because His existence is identical to His essence.
This is why the definition does not apply.
4
Connect to the broader debate
This sets up the need to discuss divine attributes and the nature of existence in Allah.
Further objections and answers will follow.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : العرض وأقسامه عند المتكلمين
محل كالصورة الجسمية الحالة في المادة وأشاروا بقولهم إذا وجدت إلى أن الوجود زائد على الماهية في الجوهر والعرض ومن ثمة لم يصدق حد الجوهر على ذات الباري .

قوله : ( وأشاروا إلخ) يعني أن قولهم إذا وجدت إلخ إشارة إلى أن الوجود الذي به موجوديته في الخارج زائد على ماهية الجوهر والعرض كما هو المتبادر إلى الفهم قوله : (لم يصدق إلخ) لأنَّ موجوديته بوجود هو نفس الماهية وإن كان الوجود المطلق زائداً عليها وبهذا اندفع ما قيل : إِنَّ حد الجوهر لا يقتضي زيادة الوجود الخاص على الماهية بل زيادة الوجود المطلق والحكماء قائلون بزيادته فلا يخرج الواجب. وقد يقال : إنَّ الماهية تدل على الكلية التزاماً فبقيد الماهية يخرج الواجب وليس بشي وليس بشي لأنه يخرج الجواهر الجزئية عن الحد ولأنَّ الكلية لازمةً للماهية بمعنى ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو الذي هو مصطلح المنطق لا الماهية بمعنى ما به الشيء هو هو الذي هو مصطلح الفلاسفة :وقيل إِن قولنا : إذا وجدت يشعر بإمكان الوجود فلا يصدق عليه تعالى وفيه إِنَّ الإشعار بالإمكان العام مسلم وهو متحقق في الواجب والإشعار بالإمكان الخاص ممنوع .

قوله : ( وأشاروا بقولهم إذا وجدت إلخ ) فيه بحث لأن حد الجوهر لا يقتضي زيادة الوجود الخاص على الماهية بل زيادة مطلق الوجود والحكماء قائلون بزيادة الوجود المطلق كما سلف في بحث الوجود فلا يخرج الواجب تعالى عن التعريف بقوله : إذا وجدت اللهم إلا أن يقال المتبادر إلى الذهن عند إطلاق نسبة الوجود إلى شيء هو وجوده الخاص ولو أخرج بقوله ماهية بناء على اعتبار الكلية في الماهية كما أشار إليه في أول الأمور العامة واقتضائها زيادة الوجود الخاص لم يكن بعيداً وقد يقال : منشأ عدم صدق هذا التعريف على الواجب تعالى أن قولنا : ماهية إذا وجدت كانت كذا مشعر بإمكان عدم الوجود فلا يصدق عليه والأصل زيادة الوجود لكن في اعتبار مثل هذا الإشعار في التعريفات بعد .
First Observatory - Second Goal: The accident and its divisions according to the theologians. A locus like the corporeal form inhering in matter, and they indicated by their saying 'when it exists' that existence is additional to quiddity in substance and accident, and therefore the definition of substance does not apply to the essence of the Necessary. His saying: ('and they indicated etc.') means that their saying 'when it exists etc.' is an indication that existence by which it is existent in the external is additional to the quiddity of substance and accident, as is the immediate understanding. His saying: ('does not apply etc.') because His existence by an existence that is the same quiddity, even though absolute existence is additional to it; and by this is repelled what was said: the definition of substance does not require the addition of specific existence to quiddity, but rather the addition of absolute existence, and the philosophers affirm its addition, so the Necessary is not excluded. And it may be said: quiddity denotes universality by implication, so by the restriction of quiddity the Necessary is excluded. But it is nothing, nothing, because it excludes particular substances from the definition, and because universality is necessary for quiddity in the sense of that by which the question 'what is it?' is answered, which is the term of logic, not quiddity in the sense of that by which a thing is identical to itself, which is the term of philosophers. And it was said: our saying 'when it exists' suggests the possibility of existence, so it does not apply to Him, Exalted. In it: the suggestion of general possibility is granted, and it is realized in the Necessary, but the suggestion of specific possibility is denied. His saying: ('and they indicated by their saying when it exists etc.') has discussion: because the definition of substance does not require the addition of specific existence to quiddity, but rather the addition of absolute existence, and the philosophers affirm the addition of absolute existence as preceded in the discussion of existence, so the Necessary, Exalted, is not excluded by the definition. Unless it is said: the immediate understanding when existence is attributed to something is its specific existence; and if He is excluded by the word 'quiddity' based on considering universality in quiddity as He indicated in the beginning of general matters, and its requiring the addition of specific existence, it would not be far-fetched. And it may be said: the origin of the definition's non-applicability to the Necessary, Exalted, is that our saying 'a quiddity when it exists is such' suggests the possibility of non-existence, so it does not apply to Him, and the principle is the addition of existence, but considering such suggestion in definitions is far.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the definition of substance and the issue of existence being additional to essence. The author first states that the definition of substance (a quiddity that when it exists is not in a subject) implies that existence is additional to the quiddity, and therefore it does not apply to Allah because His existence is identical to His essence. He then presents and answers three objections. The first objection: the definition only requires absolute existence, not specific existence, so the Necessary is not excluded. The answer: what is intended is specific existence. The second objection: quiddity implies universality, so by restricting to quiddity the Necessary is excluded, but this is rejected because it would exclude particular substances and because the universality in the definition is the logical quiddity (answer to 'what is it?'), not the metaphysical quiddity (what makes a thing itself). The third objection: the condition 'when it exists' suggests possibility, but the answer distinguishes between general possibility (which applies to the Necessary) and specific possibility (which does not). The author then adds further discussion, noting that the definition does not require specific existence but absolute existence, but the immediate understanding is specific existence, and if the definition is taken with the word 'quiddity' implying universality, it might work. Finally, a different approach: the phrase 'when it exists' suggests possibility of non-existence, but this is a far consideration in definitions.

Correction: The definition includes the condition 'when it exists', implying existence is additional to essence, which does not hold for Allah.

Checkpoint

A) Because He is not a substance
B) Because His existence is not additional to His essence
C) Because He does not exist in a subject

Key Takeaway
The theologians' definition of substance contains a condition that reveals existence is additional to essence, which is why it cannot describe Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything created has existence added to its quiddity, do you treat Allah's existence as a given or do you sometimes forget that He alone is self-sufficient? What would change if you truly felt that nothing else has existence of itself?
?
This lesson uncovers a subtle condition in the definition of substance that prevents it from applying to Allah. Are there any subtle conditions or qualifications you have unknowingly placed on your understanding of Allah? Will you examine them today?
?
The theologians were precise to protect Allah's transcendence. Are you willing to be just as precise in how you speak about Allah, even if it means challenging comfortable assumptions? What is one assumption you will correct starting now?

Objection 1: Specific vs Absolute Existence in the Definition of Substance
The author presents and answers the objection that the definition requires only absolute existence, not specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that the definition of substance only requires absolute existence, not specific existence, and its refutation.

Core Idea
The definition's requirement of absolute existence does not exclude the Necessary, but the intended meaning is specific existence.

Having established that the definition of substance implies existence is additional, an objection arises: perhaps the definition only demands absolute existence, which the philosophers also affirm as additional, so the Necessary is not excluded. This lesson examines that objection and the reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection: the definition only requires the addition of absolute existence, not specific existence.
The philosophers affirm the addition of absolute existence.
2
Understand the reply
The reply clarifies that the immediate understanding of 'when it exists' is specific existence, not absolute.
Thus the definition does imply addition of specific existence.
3
Notice the distinction
The distinction between absolute and specific existence is key.
Absolute existence is a general concept; specific existence is the particular existence of a thing.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : العرض وأقسامه عند المتكلمين
محل كالصورة الجسمية الحالة في المادة وأشاروا بقولهم إذا وجدت إلى أن الوجود زائد على الماهية في الجوهر والعرض ومن ثمة لم يصدق حد الجوهر على ذات الباري .

قوله : ( وأشاروا إلخ) يعني أن قولهم إذا وجدت إلخ إشارة إلى أن الوجود الذي به موجوديته في الخارج زائد على ماهية الجوهر والعرض كما هو المتبادر إلى الفهم قوله : (لم يصدق إلخ) لأنَّ موجوديته بوجود هو نفس الماهية وإن كان الوجود المطلق زائداً عليها وبهذا اندفع ما قيل : إِنَّ حد الجوهر لا يقتضي زيادة الوجود الخاص على الماهية بل زيادة الوجود المطلق والحكماء قائلون بزيادته فلا يخرج الواجب. وقد يقال : إنَّ الماهية تدل على الكلية التزاماً فبقيد الماهية يخرج الواجب وليس بشي وليس بشي لأنه يخرج الجواهر الجزئية عن الحد ولأنَّ الكلية لازمةً للماهية بمعنى ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو الذي هو مصطلح المنطق لا الماهية بمعنى ما به الشيء هو هو الذي هو مصطلح الفلاسفة :وقيل إِن قولنا : إذا وجدت يشعر بإمكان الوجود فلا يصدق عليه تعالى وفيه إِنَّ الإشعار بالإمكان العام مسلم وهو متحقق في الواجب والإشعار بالإمكان الخاص ممنوع .

قوله : ( وأشاروا بقولهم إذا وجدت إلخ ) فيه بحث لأن حد الجوهر لا يقتضي زيادة الوجود الخاص على الماهية بل زيادة مطلق الوجود والحكماء قائلون بزيادة الوجود المطلق كما سلف في بحث الوجود فلا يخرج الواجب تعالى عن التعريف بقوله : إذا وجدت اللهم إلا أن يقال المتبادر إلى الذهن عند إطلاق نسبة الوجود إلى شيء هو وجوده الخاص ولو أخرج بقوله ماهية بناء على اعتبار الكلية في الماهية كما أشار إليه في أول الأمور العامة واقتضائها زيادة الوجود الخاص لم يكن بعيداً وقد يقال : منشأ عدم صدق هذا التعريف على الواجب تعالى أن قولنا : ماهية إذا وجدت كانت كذا مشعر بإمكان عدم الوجود فلا يصدق عليه والأصل زيادة الوجود لكن في اعتبار مثل هذا الإشعار في التعريفات بعد .
First Observatory - Second Goal: The accident and its divisions according to the theologians. A locus like the corporeal form inhering in matter, and they indicated by their saying 'when it exists' that existence is additional to quiddity in substance and accident, and therefore the definition of substance does not apply to the essence of the Necessary. His saying: ('and they indicated etc.') means that their saying 'when it exists etc.' is an indication that existence by which it is existent in the external is additional to the quiddity of substance and accident, as is the immediate understanding. His saying: ('does not apply etc.') because His existence by an existence that is the same quiddity, even though absolute existence is additional to it; and by this is repelled what was said: the definition of substance does not require the addition of specific existence to quiddity, but rather the addition of absolute existence, and the philosophers affirm its addition, so the Necessary is not excluded. And it may be said: quiddity denotes universality by implication, so by the restriction of quiddity the Necessary is excluded. But it is nothing, nothing, because it excludes particular substances from the definition, and because universality is necessary for quiddity in the sense of that by which the question 'what is it?' is answered, which is the term of logic, not quiddity in the sense of that by which a thing is identical to itself, which is the term of philosophers. And it was said: our saying 'when it exists' suggests the possibility of existence, so it does not apply to Him, Exalted. In it: the suggestion of general possibility is granted, and it is realized in the Necessary, but the suggestion of specific possibility is denied. His saying: ('and they indicated by their saying when it exists etc.') has discussion: because the definition of substance does not require the addition of specific existence to quiddity, but rather the addition of absolute existence, and the philosophers affirm the addition of absolute existence as preceded in the discussion of existence, so the Necessary, Exalted, is not excluded by the definition. Unless it is said: the immediate understanding when existence is attributed to something is its specific existence; and if He is excluded by the word 'quiddity' based on considering universality in quiddity as He indicated in the beginning of general matters, and its requiring the addition of specific existence, it would not be far-fetched. And it may be said: the origin of the definition's non-applicability to the Necessary, Exalted, is that our saying 'a quiddity when it exists is such' suggests the possibility of non-existence, so it does not apply to Him, and the principle is the addition of existence, but considering such suggestion in definitions is far.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the definition of substance and the issue of existence being additional to essence. The author first states that the definition of substance (a quiddity that when it exists is not in a subject) implies that existence is additional to the quiddity, and therefore it does not apply to Allah because His existence is identical to His essence. He then presents and answers three objections. The first objection: the definition only requires absolute existence, not specific existence, so the Necessary is not excluded. The answer: what is intended is specific existence. The second objection: quiddity implies universality, so by restricting to quiddity the Necessary is excluded, but this is rejected because it would exclude particular substances and because the universality in the definition is the logical quiddity (answer to 'what is it?'), not the metaphysical quiddity (what makes a thing itself). The third objection: the condition 'when it exists' suggests possibility, but the answer distinguishes between general possibility (which applies to the Necessary) and specific possibility (which does not). The author then adds further discussion, noting that the definition does not require specific existence but absolute existence, but the immediate understanding is specific existence, and if the definition is taken with the word 'quiddity' implying universality, it might work. Finally, a different approach: the phrase 'when it exists' suggests possibility of non-existence, but this is a far consideration in definitions.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
الجوهر
الواجب
الوجود المطلق
الوجود الخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of substance only refers to absolute existence, so it applies to Allah.
Correction: The definition's 'when it exists' is understood as specific existence, not absolute.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim about the definition of substance?
A) It requires specific existence
B) It requires absolute existence
C) It does not require existence
D) It requires existence identical to essence

Key Takeaway
The definition of substance is understood to require the addition of specific existence, not absolute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly affirm that Allah’s existence is not like the existence of any created thing, or do you subconsciously imagine Him as a 'divine substance' possessing qualities added to His essence?
?
If your own existence is a gift added to your essence, while Allah’s existence is His very self, how does this difference shape your gratitude and dependence on Him?
?
Will you examine your heart to see if you have reduced the Creator to a mere 'being among beings'—even if the highest—or will you commit to knowing Him as the One whose existence is uniquely identical to His essence?

Objection 2: The Universality of Quiddity and Excluding the Necessary
The author presents and refutes the objection that quiddity's universality excludes the Necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that the definition's restriction to quiddity excludes the Necessary due to universality, and why this objection fails.

Core Idea
The objection that quiddity implies universality and thus excludes the Necessary is refuted because it would exclude particular substances and confuses logical quiddity with metaphysical quiddity.

Another objection is raised: the definition restricts to 'quiddity', and quiddity connotes universality, so by this restriction the Necessary (who has no quiddity) is excluded. The author points out two flaws: this would also exclude particular substances, and the universality in quiddity is from the logical meaning, not the metaphysical.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection: quiddity implies universality, so the definition's restriction to quiddity excludes the Necessary.
The objector uses the term 'quiddity' as implying universality.
2
Identify the first flaw
Notice that if quiddity implies universality, then particular substances would also be excluded, which is absurd.
The definition must apply to particular substances.
3
Identify the second flaw
Understand the distinction between logical quiddity (answer to 'what is it?') and metaphysical quiddity (what makes a thing itself).
The definition uses quiddity in the logical sense.
4
Recognize why the objection fails
The objection is invalid because it confuses these two meanings and would have unwanted consequences.
Thus the definition does not exclude the Necessary by this reasoning.

Why does the author reject the objection that quiddity implies universality?
A) Because quiddity does not imply universality at all

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to examine your own assumptions about Allah to see if any of them are based on confusing different meanings of terms—like the objector confused logical and metaphysical quiddity? Will you take a specific example from your understanding of tawhid and ensure it aligns with the source's precision?
?
When you describe Allah using attributes derived from creation, do you guard against limiting Him by your own logic, as the objector tried to exclude Him from definition? Today, will you repent of any such limiting assumption and affirm that Allah transcends all human categories?

Objection 3: The Definition Implies Possibility of Non-Existence
The author presents and answers the objection that 'when it exists' implies possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that the phrase 'when it exists' suggests possibility of non-existence, and why it does not exclude the Necessary.

Core Idea
The condition 'when it exists' only suggests general possibility, which applies to the Necessary, not specific possibility.

A third objection points out that the definition's phrase 'when it exists' suggests that existence is possible, implying that non-existence is possible. This would exclude the Necessary, who is necessary. The author replies by distinguishing between general possibility (which includes the Necessary's contingent aspect in relation to creation) and specific possibility (which implies potential non-existence).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection: 'when it exists' suggests possibility, so it cannot apply to the Necessary.
Possibility here means possibility of non-existence.
2
Understand the distinction
Understand the distinction between general possibility (الإمكان العام) and specific possibility (الإمكان الخاص).
General possibility means not impossible; specific possibility means contingent.
3
Apply to the Necessary
The Necessary has general possibility (as it is not impossible), but not specific possibility.
So the condition does not imply non-existence is possible for the Necessary.
4
Conclude that the objection is invalid
The objection fails because it conflates the two types of possibility.
The definition does not exclude the Necessary.

Key Takeaway
The definition's condition only suggests general possibility, not the possibility of non-existence, so it does not exclude the Necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you think of it as merely 'possible' in the general sense? How does the fact that your existence is contingent while Allah's is necessary affect your sense of dependence on Him? What will you do today to live in light of that dependence?
?
The objection in this lesson confused two meanings of 'possibility'. Where in your life might you be confusing general truths about Allah's attributes with specific, human-bound meanings? Will you commit to asking Him for correct understanding before assuming you know?

Further Analysis of the Definition's Implications
The author provides additional analysis and considers alternative ways to exclude the Necessary from the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand additional nuances in the discussion of whether the definition of substance implies specific or absolute existence.

Core Idea
The further discussion reiterates that the definition does not require specific existence, but the immediate understanding is such, and alternative interpretations are considered.

The author adds a final reflection, noting that strictly speaking the definition does not require the addition of specific existence, only absolute existence. However, when one attributes existence to something, the immediate understanding is specific existence. Alternative ways to exclude the Necessary are also considered, such as using the word 'quiddity' to imply universality or the suggestion of possibility. Ultimately, the author notes that such implications are far-fetched in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the further discussion
Read the passage to see the author revisiting earlier points and adding nuance.
The author notes that the definition technically requires absolute existence.
2
Understand the nuance
The definition's requirement is absolute existence, but common understanding takes it as specific.
This explains why the definition is still considered not to apply to Allah.
3
Consider the alternatives
The author discusses two alternative ways to exclude the Necessary: by the word 'quiddity' implying universality, or by the suggestion of possibility.
Both are considered but found far-fetched.
4
Conclude
The author concludes that the non-applicability is based on immediate understanding, not strict logical necessity.
This shows the importance of customary usage in definitions.

محل كالصورة الجسمية الحالة في المادة وأشاروا بقولهم إذا وجدت إلى أن الوجود زائد على الماهية في الجوهر والعرض ومن ثمة لم يصدق حد الجوهر على ذات الباري .

قوله : ( وأشاروا إلخ) يعني أن قولهم إذا وجدت إلخ إشارة إلى أن الوجود الذي به موجوديته في الخارج زائد على ماهية الجوهر والعرض كما هو المتبادر إلى الفهم قوله : (لم يصدق إلخ) لأنَّ موجوديته بوجود هو نفس الماهية وإن كان الوجود المطلق زائداً عليها وبهذا اندفع ما قيل : إِنَّ حد الجوهر لا يقتضي زيادة الوجود الخاص على الماهية بل زيادة الوجود المطلق والحكماء قائلون بزيادته فلا يخرج الواجب. وقد يقال : إنَّ الماهية تدل على الكلية التزاماً فبقيد الماهية يخرج الواجب وليس بشي وليس بشي لأنه يخرج الجواهر الجزئية عن الحد ولأنَّ الكلية لازمةً للماهية بمعنى ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو الذي هو مصطلح المنطق لا الماهية بمعنى ما به الشيء هو هو الذي هو مصطلح الفلاسفة :وقيل إِن قولنا : إذا وجدت يشعر بإمكان الوجود فلا يصدق عليه تعالى وفيه إِنَّ الإشعار بالإمكان العام مسلم وهو متحقق في الواجب والإشعار بالإمكان الخاص ممنوع .

First Observatory - Second Goal: The accident and its divisions according to the theologians. A locus like the corporeal form inhering in matter, and they indicated by their saying 'when it exists' that existence is additional to quiddity in substance and accident, and therefore the definition of substance does not apply to the essence of the Necessary. His saying: ('and they indicated etc.') means that their saying 'when it exists etc.' is an indication that existence by which it is existent in the external is additional to the quiddity of substance and accident, as is the immediate understanding. His saying: ('does not apply etc.') because His existence by an existence that is the same quiddity, even though absolute existence is additional to it; and by this is repelled what was said: the definition of substance does not require the addition of specific existence to quiddity, but rather the addition of absolute existence, and the philosophers affirm its addition, so the Necessary is not excluded. And it may be said: quiddity denotes universality by implication, so by the restriction of quiddity the Necessary is excluded. But it is nothing, nothing, because it excludes particular substances from the definition, and because universality is necessary for quiddity in the sense of that by which the question 'what is it?' is answered, which is the term of logic, not quiddity in the sense of that by which a thing is identical to itself, which is the term of philosophers. And it was said: our saying 'when it exists' suggests the possibility of existence, so it does not apply to Him, Exalted. In it: the suggestion of general possibility is granted, and it is realized in the Necessary, but the suggestion of specific possibility is denied. His saying: ('and they indicated by their saying when it exists etc.') has discussion: because the definition of substance does not require the addition of specific existence to quiddity, but rather the addition of absolute existence, and the philosophers affirm the addition of absolute existence as preceded in the discussion of existence, so the Necessary, Exalted, is not excluded by the definition. Unless it is said: the immediate understanding when existence is attributed to something is its specific existence; and if He is excluded by the word 'quiddity' based on considering universality in quiddity as He indicated in the beginning of general matters, and its requiring the addition of specific existence, it would not be far-fetched. And it may be said: the origin of the definition's non-applicability to the Necessary, Exalted, is that our saying 'a quiddity when it exists is such' suggests the possibility of non-existence, so it does not apply to Him, and the principle is the addition of existence, but considering such suggestion in definitions is far.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the definition of substance and the issue of existence being additional to essence. The author first states that the definition of substance (a quiddity that when it exists is not in a subject) implies that existence is additional to the quiddity, and therefore it does not apply to Allah because His existence is identical to His essence. He then presents and answers three objections. The first objection: the definition only requires absolute existence, not specific existence, so the Necessary is not excluded. The answer: what is intended is specific existence. The second objection: quiddity implies universality, so by restricting to quiddity the Necessary is excluded, but this is rejected because it would exclude particular substances and because the universality in the definition is the logical quiddity (answer to 'what is it?'), not the metaphysical quiddity (what makes a thing itself). The third objection: the condition 'when it exists' suggests possibility, but the answer distinguishes between general possibility (which applies to the Necessary) and specific possibility (which does not). The author then adds further discussion, noting that the definition does not require specific existence but absolute existence, but the immediate understanding is specific existence, and if the definition is taken with the word 'quiddity' implying universality, it might work. Finally, a different approach: the phrase 'when it exists' suggests possibility of non-existence, but this is a far consideration in definitions.

Consensus and the Mutakallimun's Argument
Sanusi states the consensus and the mutakallimun's spatial argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun argue that an accident cannot transfer from one substrate to another, based on the definition of transfer as spatial change.

Core Idea
Transfer requires spatial occupancy; accidents lack it.

The author begins by stating that rational people agree that accidents do not transfer. He then presents the first argument, from the mutakallimun: transfer is only conceivable for beings that occupy space. Since accidents do not occupy space, they cannot transfer. This lesson examines that argument and its foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's style and the statement of consensus.
The passage starts with the objective title.
2
Identify the definition of transfer
Find where the author defines transfer. Note that it is defined as 'obtaining a thing in a space after it was in another space.'
This definition is key to the argument.
3
Understand why accidents are excluded
The argument concludes that since accidents do not occupy space, they cannot undergo such transfer. Ask why this must be so.
The mutakallimun assume transfer is essentially spatial.

The First Observatory - The Fifth Objective: That the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. (It is permissible) [that the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another] by analogy to the transfer of a body from one place to another. This is a judgment upon whose correctness the rational people have agreed. (According to the mutakallimun, because transfer is only conceivable in that which occupies space), and that is because transfer is the obtaining of a thing in a space after it was in another space, and this meaning is only realized in that which occupies space, and the accident does not occupy space. (And in it there is consideration, for that transfer interpreted as mentioned is the transfer of the substance from one place to another. As for the transfer of the accident which is our subject, it is that a particular accident should subsist in a substrate after having subsisted in another substrate. And this is not something inconceivable for an accident; rather, its negation requires a proof. It cannot be said: 'During transfer, it is either in the first substrate or the second, and both are invalid, because its being in the first substrate is a stability in it prior to transfer from it, and its being in the second substrate is an establishment in it posterior to transfer to it; or it is in another substrate, and the discourse returns to its transfer to this substrate, and that same difficulty arises.' For we say: It is possible that the transfer of the accident be instantaneous, not gradual, so that the moment of its separation is the same as the moment of its conjunction with another substrate. (And according to the philosophers, because its individuation) i.e., the individuation of the particular entity (is not due to its essence and quiddity nor to their necessary concomitants, otherwise its species would be confined to its individual, nor due to its substrate.) The accident. His saying: (As for the transfer of the accident which etc.) i.e., the transfer that is impossible for the accident. His saying: (And as for another substrate etc.) means during the state of transfer in a substrate other than the one preceding it and the one posterior to it, the transfer occurred in it. His saying: (And the discourse returns etc.) by saying: during the transfer to this substrate, either it is in the first substrate which is prior, or in this substrate which is posterior to it, and it cannot be said that it is in another substrate other than this one, for then there would follow the existence of infinite substrates during the transfer from substrate to substrate. Place. His saying: (It is possible that it be etc.) means it is possible that the existence in the first substrate be in an instant and the existence in the second substrate be in a second instant, so that its separation from the first substrate is the same as its conjunction with the second substrate; thus the transfer of the accident is valid without the necessity of its existence without a substrate. And similarly, the case of the transfer of the body from one place to another according to the method of the mutakallimun, for motion according to them is nothing but a second existence in a second place. As for the method of the philosophers, its explanation will come that it is one continuous thing, non-fixed in essence, coinciding with the distance which is from. His saying: (Because transfer is only conceivable in that which occupies space) i.e., essentially, and what is meant by the obtaining mentioned in the interpretation of transfer is obtaining essentially as well, so it cannot be objected: why is it not sufficient for it to occupy space derivatively? His saying: (For we say: It is possible that the transfer of the accident be instantaneous) and similarly the transfer of the substance according to the mutakallimun, because they do not require motion to be in a distance; rather, if a part transfers from its place to another part that meets it, motion is realized. Hence they said: exiting the first space is the same as entering the second, as will be verified in the investigations of modes (akwan). As for according to the philosophers, the transfer of the substance is gradual, and it is in the state of transfer in the distance, as you will come to know their doctrine and what is objected to them, God willing. His saying: (Not due to its essence) i.e., its essence does not necessitate its individuation with complete necessity, as indicated in the twelfth objective of the eighth observatory.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objective: that an accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. The author begins by stating that this is a universally accepted judgment among rational people. He then provides two main arguments: the argument of the mutakallimun and the argument of the philosophers. The mutakallimun argue that transfer is only conceivable for beings that occupy space (mutahayyiz), because transfer is defined as obtaining something in a space after having been in another space. Since accidents do not occupy space, they cannot transfer. However, the author notes a consideration: this definition of transfer applies to substances, not to accidents. The transfer of an accident would be the same accident subsisting in a second substrate after having subsisted in a first, which is not inherently inconceivable; a separate proof is needed. The author then addresses a potential objection: if the accident is in the first substrate before transfer, and in the second after, then during transfer it would be nowhere, which is impossible. The answer given is that the transfer could be instantaneous, so the moment of leaving the first substrate is the same as the moment of entering the second, avoiding any period of non-existence. The philosophers argue from individuation (tashakhkhush): the individuation of a particular accident is not due to its essence or its necessary concomitants, nor to its substrate. Therefore, if it transferred, it would lose its individuation and become a different accident. The commentator elaborates on these points, explaining the definitions and noting that the mutakallimun allow instantaneous transfer for both accidents and bodies, while philosophers view transfer as gradual. The passage concludes with a clarification that 'transfer is only conceivable in that which occupies space' means essential occupancy, not derivative.

Key Terms
العرض (al-‘arad, accident)
الانتقال (al-intiqal, transfer)
المتحيز (al-mutahayyiz, that which occupies space)
المتكلمون (al-mutakallimun, theologians)

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun argue that because transfer is defined as spatial change, and accidents are not spatial, accidents cannot transfer.

?

The Objection and Instantaneous Transfer
Sanusi presents an objection and answers it with instantaneous transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that during transfer the accident would exist without a substrate, and the answer that transfer can be instantaneous.

Core Idea
Instantaneous transfer avoids the problem of accident existence without a substrate.

After presenting the mutakallimun's argument, the author notes a consideration: the definition of transfer as spatial change applies only to substances. He then presents a powerful objection: if an accident were to transfer, it would have to exist without a substrate during the transition. The answer is that transfer can be instantaneous, so there is no intermediate state.

Guided Path
1
Read the consideration
Notice that the author points out the earlier definition fits substances, not accidents. The transfer of an accident is a different concept.
This sets up the need for a separate proof.
2
Understand the objection
The objection argues that the accident must be either in the first substrate, the second, or another, each leading to impossibility.
This is a classic dilemma.
3
See the answer
The author responds that transfer can be instantaneous, so the moment of leaving and arriving coincide, avoiding a substrate-less state.
Instantaneous transfer is the key.

للوازمها ( وإلا انحصر نوعه في شخصه ولا لما محله العرض قوله : ( وأما انتقال العرض الذي إلخ) أي الانتقال المحال على العرض. قوله : ( وأما في محل آخر إلخ) يعني في حال الانتقال في محل سوى المحل السابق عليه والمحل المتأخر عنه وقع الانتقال فيه . قوله : ( ويعود الكلام إلخ ) بأن يقال حال الانتقال إلى هذا المحل إما في المحل الأول وهو سابق أو في هذا المحل وهو متأخر عنه ولا يمكن أن يقال : إنه في محل آخر سوى هذا المحل فإنه يلزم وجود محال غير متناهية حال الانتقال من محل إلى محل . مکان قوله : (جاز أن يكون إلخ ) يعني يجوز أن يكون الكون في المحل الأول في آن والكون فـ المحل الثاني في آن ثان فيكون أنَّ مفارقته من المحل هو أنَّ مقارنته مع المحل الثاني فيصح الانتقال على العرض من غير لزوم وجوده بدون المحل وهكذا الحال في انتقال الجسم من إلى آخر على طريقة المتكلمين فإنَّ الحركة عندهم ليس إلا كون ثان في مكان ثان وأما طريقة الحكماء فسيجيء بيانه أنها أمر متصل واحد غير قار الذات منطبقة على المسافة التي هي من قوله : ( لأن الانتقال إنما يتصور في المتحيز ) أي بالذات والمراد بالحصول المذكور في تفسير الانتقال هو الحصول بالذات أيضاً فلا يرد أنه لم لا يكفي التحيز التبعي . قوله : (لأنا نقول جاز أن يكون انتقال العرض (دفعياً وكذلك انتقال الجوهر عند المتكلمين لأنهم لا يشترطون في الحركة أن يكون في مسافة بل إذا انتقل جزء من مكانه إلى جزء آخر يلاقيه يتحقق الحركة ولذا قالوا إِنَّ الخروج عن الحيز الأول عين الدخول في الثاني كما سيحقق في مباحث الأكوان وأما عند الفلاسفة فائتقال الجوهر تدريجي وهو حال الانتقال في المسافة كما ستطلع على مذهبهم وما يرد عليهم إن شاء الله تعالى . قوله : (ليس بذاته أي ليس ذاته مقتضية لتشخصه اقتضاء تاماً كما أشار إليه في المقصد الثاني عشر من المرصد الثامن.
The First Observatory - The Fifth Objective: That the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. (It is permissible) [that the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another] by analogy to the transfer of a body from one place to another. This is a judgment upon whose correctness the rational people have agreed. (According to the mutakallimun, because transfer is only conceivable in that which occupies space), and that is because transfer is the obtaining of a thing in a space after it was in another space, and this meaning is only realized in that which occupies space, and the accident does not occupy space. (And in it there is consideration, for that transfer interpreted as mentioned is the transfer of the substance from one place to another. As for the transfer of the accident which is our subject, it is that a particular accident should subsist in a substrate after having subsisted in another substrate. And this is not something inconceivable for an accident; rather, its negation requires a proof. It cannot be said: 'During transfer, it is either in the first substrate or the second, and both are invalid, because its being in the first substrate is a stability in it prior to transfer from it, and its being in the second substrate is an establishment in it posterior to transfer to it; or it is in another substrate, and the discourse returns to its transfer to this substrate, and that same difficulty arises.' For we say: It is possible that the transfer of the accident be instantaneous, not gradual, so that the moment of its separation is the same as the moment of its conjunction with another substrate. (And according to the philosophers, because its individuation) i.e., the individuation of the particular entity (is not due to its essence and quiddity nor to their necessary concomitants, otherwise its species would be confined to its individual, nor due to its substrate.) The accident. His saying: (As for the transfer of the accident which etc.) i.e., the transfer that is impossible for the accident. His saying: (And as for another substrate etc.) means during the state of transfer in a substrate other than the one preceding it and the one posterior to it, the transfer occurred in it. His saying: (And the discourse returns etc.) by saying: during the transfer to this substrate, either it is in the first substrate which is prior, or in this substrate which is posterior to it, and it cannot be said that it is in another substrate other than this one, for then there would follow the existence of infinite substrates during the transfer from substrate to substrate. Place. His saying: (It is possible that it be etc.) means it is possible that the existence in the first substrate be in an instant and the existence in the second substrate be in a second instant, so that its separation from the first substrate is the same as its conjunction with the second substrate; thus the transfer of the accident is valid without the necessity of its existence without a substrate. And similarly, the case of the transfer of the body from one place to another according to the method of the mutakallimun, for motion according to them is nothing but a second existence in a second place. As for the method of the philosophers, its explanation will come that it is one continuous thing, non-fixed in essence, coinciding with the distance which is from. His saying: (Because transfer is only conceivable in that which occupies space) i.e., essentially, and what is meant by the obtaining mentioned in the interpretation of transfer is obtaining essentially as well, so it cannot be objected: why is it not sufficient for it to occupy space derivatively? His saying: (For we say: It is possible that the transfer of the accident be instantaneous) and similarly the transfer of the substance according to the mutakallimun, because they do not require motion to be in a distance; rather, if a part transfers from its place to another part that meets it, motion is realized. Hence they said: exiting the first space is the same as entering the second, as will be verified in the investigations of modes (akwan). As for according to the philosophers, the transfer of the substance is gradual, and it is in the state of transfer in the distance, as you will come to know their doctrine and what is objected to them, God willing. His saying: (Not due to its essence) i.e., its essence does not necessitate its individuation with complete necessity, as indicated in the twelfth objective of the eighth observatory.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objective: that an accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. The author begins by stating that this is a universally accepted judgment among rational people. He then provides two main arguments: the argument of the mutakallimun and the argument of the philosophers. The mutakallimun argue that transfer is only conceivable for beings that occupy space (mutahayyiz), because transfer is defined as obtaining something in a space after having been in another space. Since accidents do not occupy space, they cannot transfer. However, the author notes a consideration: this definition of transfer applies to substances, not to accidents. The transfer of an accident would be the same accident subsisting in a second substrate after having subsisted in a first, which is not inherently inconceivable; a separate proof is needed. The author then addresses a potential objection: if the accident is in the first substrate before transfer, and in the second after, then during transfer it would be nowhere, which is impossible. The answer given is that the transfer could be instantaneous, so the moment of leaving the first substrate is the same as the moment of entering the second, avoiding any period of non-existence. The philosophers argue from individuation (tashakhkhush): the individuation of a particular accident is not due to its essence or its necessary concomitants, nor to its substrate. Therefore, if it transferred, it would lose its individuation and become a different accident. The commentator elaborates on these points, explaining the definitions and noting that the mutakallimun allow instantaneous transfer for both accidents and bodies, while philosophers view transfer as gradual. The passage concludes with a clarification that 'transfer is only conceivable in that which occupies space' means essential occupancy, not derivative.

Key Terms
العرض (al-‘arad, accident)
الانتقال (al-intiqal, transfer)
الدفعي (al-daf‘i, instantaneous)
المتكلمون (al-mutakallimun, theologians)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think transfer must be gradual, so the accident would exist without a substrate mid-transfer.
Correction: The passage shows instantaneous transfer is possible, avoiding any intermediate state.

Checkpoint
How do the mutakallimun answer the objection that the accident would have to exist without a substrate during transfer?
A) They deny that transfer requires a substrate.
B) They argue that the accident exists in the medium.
C) They allow instantaneous transfer, so leaving and arriving are simultaneous.
D) They claim the objection is invalid because accidents can exist without substrates.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the accident would exist without a substrate during transfer is answered by allowing instantaneous transfer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that change can happen without any gap—instantaneously. In your own life, do you assume that big changes require time and gradual steps, or do you truly believe that Allah can transform your heart in an instant? What would change if you lived with that certainty?
?
The objection in this passage seems logical, yet it is answered by affirming instantaneous transfer. When you face intellectual doubts about faith, do you quickly assume the objection is unanswerable, or do you trust that a deeper truth exists? Will you commit to seeking that truth rather than settling for apparent contradictions?
?
If Allah’s power can transfer an accident without any temporal gap, what does that imply about how quickly He can respond to your du‘a’ or change your situation? Are you living with the urgency of that reality, or do you still think in terms of slow causality? How can you realign your expectations today?

Divine Attributes

The Philosophers' Individuation Argument
Sanusi presents the philosophers' argument about individuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers argue against transfer of accidents based on individuation (tashakhkhush).

Core Idea
An accident's identity is tied to its substrate; if it transferred, it would become a different accident.

The author now presents the second main argument, from the philosophers. They focus on individuation: the particular identity of an accident is not derived from its essence or its necessary concomitants, nor from its substrate. If the accident were to transfer, it would lose its individuation and become a different accident. Therefore, transfer is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the brief Arabic passage. Notice that it states that an accident's individuation is not due to its essence, its necessary properties, or its substrate.
This is a condensed statement.
2
Understand what individuation means
Individuation (tashakhkhush) is what makes a particular accident this specific accident. The passage denies it comes from the accident's own nature or from the substrate it inheres in.
If it came from the substrate, moving would change it.
3
See why transfer would change identity
If the accident were to transfer, its individuation would have to be preserved. But since it is not intrinsic nor from the substrate, it could not be maintained. Thus transfer is impossible.
The passage also warns against thinking individuation is from essence, else all accidents of that type would be identical.

Misconception: One might think an accident's identity is independent of its substrate and can persist across substrates.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what would happen if an accident's individuation were due to its essence?
A) Every accident of that type would be the same individual.
B) It could not change substrates.
C) It would be eternal.
D) It would be impossible for accidents to exist.

Key Takeaway
Philosophers argue that since an accident's individuation is not intrinsic or from its substrate, transferring would change its identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your best deeds are accidents that cannot exist independently of you, what claim to them do you have after death? Are they still yours, or do they become something else?
?
The philosophers argue that an accident's identity is not from its essence. That means your actions are not inherently good or evil—they depend entirely on the substrate. Does this realization strip you of pride in your 'righteous' deeds, or does it make you cling more to the One who sustains your very existence?
?
How does the impossibility of an accident transferring challenge your sense of personal continuity? If your identity as a believer is built on accidents of faith, what truly remains constant across time and place? Will you rely on God's preservation or on your own supposed fixed nature?

The Accident Does Not Transfer: Initial Argument and Objections
Initial proof against accident transfer with separate cause objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial proof that an accident cannot transfer from one substrate to another, based on the impossibility of an absolute existence causing a particular individuation.

Core Idea
An absolute existence cannot cause a particular individuation without circularity.

The author starts by stating the claim and providing a logical argument: if existence is taken absolutely, it cannot determine a particular substrate; if taken particularly, it would lead to a circle. He then addresses an objection that a free agent could have a specific relation to a particular individuation, but argues that this would make the accident independent of the substrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the compressed style of the original.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the core logical move: taking existence absolutely vs. particularly, and why neither allows transfer. The key is the circularity if reversed.
3
Understand the separate cause objection
The objection says a free agent (like God) could have a specific relation to a particular substrate. See why the author responds that this fails for accidents with only one individual.
This shows the author is aware of a potential counterexample.
4
Reflect on the limitation
The argument is not universal: it does not apply to accidents whose species has only one individual. Why? Because then individuation might be intrinsic.
This nuance is important for completeness.
