ومقتضية إياه لذاتها ( أو ) إلى محل ( غير معين ولا وجود له في الخارج لأن كل موجود في الخارج فهو متعين في نفسه (فيلزم ) حينئذ ( أن لا يوجد العرض) في الخارج لانتفاء المحل الذي يحتاج هو إليه وهذا باطل قطعاً فتعين الأول وامتنع الانتقال وهو المطلوب ) وفيه نظر إذ قد يحتاج ( العرض المعين ( إلى محل بلا شرط معين قوله : ( لم يكن علة لتشخص معين ) إذ المبهم لا يجوز أن يكون علة فاعلية للمعين . قوله : ( وإن أخذ معيناً فكذلك إلخ) أي إن أخذ الوجود الخارجي المعين فلا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الوجود موجوداً في الخارج وإلا لزم أن يكون تشخص العرض بما حل فيه وقد أبطلناه فيكون أمراً اعتبارياً فنعينه إنما يكون بتعين العرض الذي قام به فلو كان علة لتعينه لزم الدور . قوله : ( ويتجه إلخ ) هذا الاتجاه إنما يتجه لو وجد عرض منحصر نوعه في شخصه قوله : ( العرض يحتاج إلى المحل وإلا لم يكن عرضاً وربما يجاب بأنه يجوز أن يحتاج إلى محل معين لا من حيث أنه هذا المعين فيجوز الانتقال عليه وفيه أنه يلزم التوارد على سبيل البدل . الجواز . قوله : ( إذ قد يحتاج أي يجوز أن يحتاج لأنه اللازم من الدليل المذكور ولأنه مانع يكفيه يجوز مفارقته عنه كما في المادة بالنسبة إلى الصورة فإن تعينها إنما يحتاج إلى الصورة من حيث هي صورة ولذلك جاز مفارقتها من الصورة والجواب ما يشير إليه الشارح في تعريفات الهيولى من أن الواحد بالشخص لا بد أن تكون علته واحدة بالشخص فلا يعقل أن يكون علة العرض المشخص محلا مطلقا واحتياج الهيولى إلى الصورة في البقاء لا في التشخص بل الأمر بالعكس نعم يشكل حينئذ ما ذكره في بحث التعين كما أشرنا هناك . قوله: (إذ يجوز أن يكون له نسبة خاصة إلى تشخص معين قيل : لا يجوز أن يكون المنفصل علة لتشخص العرض لأنه يكون العرض حينئذ مكتفيا في تشخصه ووجوده بغير الموضوع والمكتفي فيهما بغير المحل لا يفتقر إلى المحل فيكون مستغنياً عنه وهو باطل. قوله : (لا يطرد في عرض ينحصر نوعه في شخصه إذ يجوز أن يكون تشخصه لذاته وماهيته أو للوازمها . التعين أي إلى محل مطلق غير مقيد بالتعين ( وإنه أعم من المعين) الذي قيد بالتعين ( فيوجد ( ذلك المطلق المأخوذ بلا شرط التعين في كل معين من المعينات ( لا ) إلى محل مقيد ( بشرط عدم التعين) حتى يمتنع وجوده في الخارج فيلزم أن لا يوجد العرض فيه وإنما قلنا : إنه يحتاج إلى المحل المطلق عن التعين ولا يحتاج إلى المقيد بعدم التعين ( إذ لا يلزم من عدم اعتبار التعين في المحل الذي يحتاج إليه العرض المعين ( اعتبار عدم التعين فيه كما قد علمته من أن الماهية المطلقة التي لم يعتبر فيها وجود عوارضها ولم تقيد به أعم من الماهية المخلوطة المقيدة به الموجودة في الخارج ومن المجردة المقيدة بعدمها المستحيل في الخارج وجودها ( وأيضاً فهو أي ما ذكرتم من الدليل ) وارد في الجسم بالنسبة إلى الحيز) فيقال الجسم يحتاج في كونه متحيزاً إلى الحيز بالضرورة فإما أن يحتاج إلى حيز معين أو غير معين والثاني باطل لأن غير المعين لا وجود له فيلزم أن لا يوجد الجسم المتحيز فتعين الأول فلا يجوز انتقال الجسم عن الحيز المعين إلى غيره فانتقض دليلكم وما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا ) فإن قيل هذا الذي ذكرتموه من امتناع الانتقال على العرض ( إنكار للحس فإنَّ رائحة التفاح تنتقل منه إلى ما يجاوره والحرارة تنتقل من النار إلى ما يماسها كما يشهد به الحس ) فالجواب أن الحاصل في المحل الثاني ) وهو المجاور أو المماس ( شخص آخر من الرائحة أو الحرارة مماثل للأول الحاصل في التفاح أو النار ) يحدثه الفاعل المختار عندنا بطريق العادة عقيب المجاورة أو المماسة ( أو يفيض ) ذلك الشخص الآخر على المحل الثاني ( من العقل الفعال ) عند الحكماء بطريق الوجوب ) لاستعداد يحصل له من المجاورة ) أو المماسة
The First Observatory - The Fifth Aim: That the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. If taken absolutely, it would not be a cause for a particular individuation; and if taken particularly, likewise, because the individuation of existences among individuals of a specific essence is only by the individuations of those individuals. If reversed, there would be a circle. Yes, an objection is raised against the proof: We do not accept that the relation of the separate cause to the whole is equal, because it is possible that it has a specific relation to a particular individuation, especially if the separate cause is a free agent, for it can choose what it wills. And it is also directed against it that it does not apply universally to an accident whose species is confined to its individual. (And it may be said) in proving the impossibility of transfer: The accident needs the substrate necessarily. So either the particular accident needs a particular substrate and does not leave it, because the particularity of that particular accident is connected to that particular substrate and necessitates it by its essence, or it needs a non-particular substrate which has no existence in the external world, because everything existing externally is particular in itself. Then it would follow that the accident does not exist externally due to the non-existence of the substrate it needs, and this is absolutely false. So the first is determined, and transfer is impossible, which is what is sought. And there is a consideration: Indeed, the particular accident may need a substrate without the condition of particularity. His saying: '(It would not be a cause for a particular individuation)' because the indeterminate cannot be an efficient cause for the particular. His saying: '(And if taken particularly, likewise, etc.)' means: If the particular external existence is taken, then that existence cannot exist externally, otherwise it would entail that the individuation of the accident is by what it inheres in, and we have refuted that, so it would be a mental consideration. So its individuation is only by the individuation of the substrate it subsists in; if it were a cause for its individuation, there would be a circle. His saying: '(And it is also directed against it, etc.)' This direction only applies if there exists an accident whose species is confined to its individual. His saying: '(The accident needs the substrate, otherwise it would not be an accident, and perhaps it is answered that it is permissible for it to need a particular substrate not insofar as it is this particular, so transfer would be permissible upon it, and therein it entails occurrence in alternation)' Possibility. His saying: '(Indeed, it may need)' means it may need, because it is necessitated by the aforementioned proof, and because it is a preventative that suffices for it to be possible for it to leave it, like in matter in relation to form, for its individuation only needs the form insofar as it is form, and therefore it is permissible for it to separate from the form. The answer is what the commentator points to in the definitions of prime matter: that the individual unit must have a single cause, so it is not rationally conceivable that the cause of the individuated accident is an absolute substrate. And the need of prime matter for form is for subsistence, not for individuation; rather, the opposite is true. Yes, then there is difficulty in what he mentioned in the discussion of individuation, as we indicated there. His saying: '(Because it is possible that it has a specific relation to a particular individuation)' It is said: It is not permissible for the separate cause to be the cause for the individuation of the accident, because then the accident would be self-sufficient in its individuation and existence without the subject, and one who is self-sufficient in both without the substrate does not need the substrate, so it would be independent of it, which is false. His saying: '(It does not apply universally to an accident whose species is confined to its individual)' because it is possible that its individuation is by itself and its essence or by its necessary concomitants. Individuation means: to an absolute substrate not restricted by individuation, and it is more general than the particular which is restricted by individuation. So that absolute taken without the condition of individuation exists in every particular of the particulars. Not to a substrate restricted by the condition of non-individuation, so that its existence externally is impossible, then it would follow that the accident does not exist in it. Rather we say: It needs the substrate absolute of individuation and does not need the one restricted to non-individuation, because from the non-consideration of individuation in the substrate that the particular accident needs, it does not necessitate the consideration of non-individuation in it, as you have known: that the absolute essence in which the existence of its accidents is not considered and not restricted by it is more general than the mixed essence restricted by it which exists externally, and the abstract essence restricted by its non-existence is impossible to exist externally. (And also, it, i.e. the proof you mentioned, applies to the body in relation to place): It is said: The body needs place in its being localized necessarily. So either it needs a particular place or a non-particular place, and the second is false because the non-particular has no existence, so it would follow that the localized body does not exist. So the first is determined, and transfer of the body from its particular place to another is not permissible. Thus your proof is refuted, and whatever your answer is, it is our answer. If it is said: This that you have mentioned regarding the impossibility of transfer for the accident is a denial of sense perception, for the smell of an apple transfers from it to what is adjacent to it, and heat transfers from fire to what contacts it, as sense perception witnesses. Then the answer: That which occurs in the second substrate, which is the adjacent or contacting, is another individual of smell or heat similar to the first occurring in the apple or fire, which the free agent brings about, according to us, by way of habit following adjacency or contact; or that other individual flows onto the second substrate from the Active Intellect, according to the philosophers, by way of necessity, due to a preparedness obtained from adjacency or contact.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth aim of the first observatory: proving that an accident cannot transfer from one substrate to another. It begins by stating that if existence is taken absolutely, it cannot be a cause for a particular individuation; if taken particularly, likewise, because individuation comes from the individuations of individuals, leading to a circle if reversed. An objection is raised: we do not accept that the separate cause's relation to the whole is equal; it may have a specific relation to a particular individuation, especially if the agent is free. Also, the proof does not apply to accidents whose species is confined to one individual. Then a proof for impossibility is given: an accident necessarily needs a substrate. If it needs a particular substrate, it cannot leave it because its particularity is tied to that substrate. If it needs a non-particular substrate, that substrate has no external existence, so the accident would not exist. Thus it must need a particular substrate, and transfer is impossible. The passage then notes a consideration: the particular accident may only need a substrate without the condition of particularity. The commentary explains each phrase: the indeterminate cannot be a cause; taking particular existence externally leads to a circle; the objection about confined species is only relevant if such an accident exists; the need for a substrate is necessary; the answer about needing a non-particular substrate leads to alternation. The commentator also draws an analogy with matter and form: matter's individuation needs form as form, not as particular, and the individual unit must have a single cause. Further objections are addressed: the separate cause cannot be the cause of individuation because then the accident would be independent of the substrate, which is false. The passage continues with an objection that the same proof applies to bodies and place, to which the same answer is given. Finally, a sense perception objection is raised: smell and heat appear to transfer. The answer is that what occurs in the second substrate is a new similar individual, created by the free agent habitually or by the Active Intellect due to preparedness from contact. This entire passage systematically defends the doctrine that accidents do not transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the separate cause (e.g., God) can directly transfer an accident by willing a particular relation.
Correction: The passage argues that even if that relation is possible, it would still not involve transfer because the accident would need to be independent of the substrate, which is impossible for an accident.

Checkpoint
According to the initial argument, why cannot an absolute existence cause an accident’s individuation?
A) Because absolute existence does not exist in the external world.
B) Because an absolute existence does not have the power to pick out a particular substrate.
C) Because individuation depends on the substrate's own individuation, not on an absolute existence.
D) Because the separate cause prevents it.

Key Takeaway
An absolute existence cannot serve as the cause for a particular accident substrate, and even a separate free agent does not enable transfer without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because God is all-powerful, He can do anything, even what seems logically contradictory? How does this passage challenge you to accept that God's power operates within a framework of wisdom and necessity? How will you adjust your trust in God's consistent order today?
?
When you face a confusing theological question, do you tend to settle for a simple answer without digging deeper? This passage presents a detailed logical argument. What does this teach you about your responsibility to seek understanding in matters of faith? Where in your life have you been too lazy to think deeply about God?
?
The objection suggests that God, as a free agent, might have special relations to particular things. How does this make you feel about God's involvement in your unique circumstances? Does your prayer life reflect a belief that God is intimately aware of your specific needs, or do you treat Him as a distant, general force? What will you do differently?

Divine Attributes
advanced
6 min
The Main Proof: Accident Needs a Particular Substrate
Main proof for accident's need for particular substrate and objection from body transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that an accident must need a particular substrate, and why it cannot leave it.

Core Idea
If an accident needed a non-particular substrate, that substrate would not exist, so the accident must need a particular one.

This lesson presents the central proof: an accident necessarily requires a substrate. If it required a particular substrate, it cannot leave it because its particularity is tied to that substrate. If it required a non-particular substrate, that substrate has no external existence, so the accident would not exist. Hence, it must need a particular substrate and cannot transfer. The passage also addresses a consideration that the accident may need a substrate without the condition of particularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the two options given for the accident's need for substrate.
The proof is a dilemma: particular or non-particular substrate.
2
Understand why non-particular is impossible
The argument states that every existing thing in the external world is particular, so a non-particular substrate does not exist. Therefore, the accident would not exist if it needed such a substrate.
3
Consider the nuance about 'substrate without condition'
The passage introduces a consideration: maybe the accident only needs a substrate without the condition of particularity. Think about how this could be answered.
This leads to the idea that even a non-restricted substrate must still be existent, hence particular.
4
Study the body-place objection
An opponent uses the same logic to argue that a body cannot change place, which is false. See how the author implies that the analogy is flawed.
The key difference: place is not essential to body's existence, but substrate is essential to accident's existence.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : أن العرض لا ينتقل من محل إلى محل أخذ مطلقاً لم يكن علة لتشخص معين وإن أخذ معيناً فكذلك لأن تعين الوجودات في أفراد ماهية نوعية إنما يكون بتعينات تلك الأفراد فلو عكس دار The First Observatory - The Fifth Aim: That the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. If taken absolutely, it would not be a cause for a particular individuation; and if taken particularly, likewise, because the individuation of existences among individuals of a specific essence is only by the individuations of those individuals. If reversed, there would be a circle. Yes, an objection is raised against the proof: We do not accept that the relation of the separate cause to the whole is equal, because it is possible that it has a specific relation to a particular individuation, especially if the separate cause is a free agent, for it can choose what it wills. And it is also directed against it that it does not apply universally to an accident whose species is confined to its individual. (And it may be said) in proving the impossibility of transfer: The accident needs the substrate necessarily. So either the particular accident needs a particular substrate and does not leave it, because the particularity of that particular accident is connected to that particular substrate and necessitates it by its essence, or it needs a non-particular substrate which has no existence in the external world, because everything existing externally is particular in itself. Then it would follow that the accident does not exist externally due to the non-existence of the substrate it needs, and this is absolutely false. So the first is determined, and transfer is impossible, which is what is sought. And there is a consideration: Indeed, the particular accident may need a substrate without the condition of particularity. His saying: '(It would not be a cause for a particular individuation)' because the indeterminate cannot be an efficient cause for the particular. His saying: '(And if taken particularly, likewise, etc.)' means: If the particular external existence is taken, then that existence cannot exist externally, otherwise it would entail that the individuation of the accident is by what it inheres in, and we have refuted that, so it would be a mental consideration. So its individuation is only by the individuation of the substrate it subsists in; if it were a cause for its individuation, there would be a circle. His saying: '(And it is also directed against it, etc.)' This direction only applies if there exists an accident whose species is confined to its individual. His saying: '(The accident needs the substrate, otherwise it would not be an accident, and perhaps it is answered that it is permissible for it to need a particular substrate not insofar as it is this particular, so transfer would be permissible upon it, and therein it entails occurrence in alternation)' Possibility. His saying: '(Indeed, it may need)' means it may need, because it is necessitated by the aforementioned proof, and because it is a preventative that suffices for it to be possible for it to leave it, like in matter in relation to form, for its individuation only needs the form insofar as it is form, and therefore it is permissible for it to separate from the form. The answer is what the commentator points to in the definitions of prime matter: that the individual unit must have a single cause, so it is not rationally conceivable that the cause of the individuated accident is an absolute substrate. And the need of prime matter for form is for subsistence, not for individuation; rather, the opposite is true. Yes, then there is difficulty in what he mentioned in the discussion of individuation, as we indicated there. His saying: '(Because it is possible that it has a specific relation to a particular individuation)' It is said: It is not permissible for the separate cause to be the cause for the individuation of the accident, because then the accident would be self-sufficient in its individuation and existence without the subject, and one who is self-sufficient in both without the substrate does not need the substrate, so it would be independent of it, which is false. His saying: '(It does not apply universally to an accident whose species is confined to its individual)' because it is possible that its individuation is by itself and its essence or by its necessary concomitants. Individuation means: to an absolute substrate not restricted by individuation, and it is more general than the particular which is restricted by individuation. So that absolute taken without the condition of individuation exists in every particular of the particulars. Not to a substrate restricted by the condition of non-individuation, so that its existence externally is impossible, then it would follow that the accident does not exist in it. Rather we say: It needs the substrate absolute of individuation and does not need the one restricted to non-individuation, because from the non-consideration of individuation in the substrate that the particular accident needs, it does not necessitate the consideration of non-individuation in it, as you have known: that the absolute essence in which the existence of its accidents is not considered and not restricted by it is more general than the mixed essence restricted by it which exists externally, and the abstract essence restricted by its non-existence is impossible to exist externally. (And also, it, i.e. the proof you mentioned, applies to the body in relation to place): It is said: The body needs place in its being localized necessarily. So either it needs a particular place or a non-particular place, and the second is false because the non-particular has no existence, so it would follow that the localized body does not exist. So the first is determined, and transfer of the body from its particular place to another is not permissible. Thus your proof is refuted, and whatever your answer is, it is our answer. If it is said: This that you have mentioned regarding the impossibility of transfer for the accident is a denial of sense perception, for the smell of an apple transfers from it to what is adjacent to it, and heat transfers from fire to what contacts it, as sense perception witnesses. Then the answer: That which occurs in the second substrate, which is the adjacent or contacting, is another individual of smell or heat similar to the first occurring in the apple or fire, which the free agent brings about, according to us, by way of habit following adjacency or contact; or that other individual flows onto the second substrate from the Active Intellect, according to the philosophers, by way of necessity, due to a preparedness obtained from adjacency or contact.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth aim of the first observatory: proving that an accident cannot transfer from one substrate to another. It begins by stating that if existence is taken absolutely, it cannot be a cause for a particular individuation; if taken particularly, likewise, because individuation comes from the individuations of individuals, leading to a circle if reversed. An objection is raised: we do not accept that the separate cause's relation to the whole is equal; it may have a specific relation to a particular individuation, especially if the agent is free. Also, the proof does not apply to accidents whose species is confined to one individual. Then a proof for impossibility is given: an accident necessarily needs a substrate. If it needs a particular substrate, it cannot leave it because its particularity is tied to that substrate. If it needs a non-particular substrate, that substrate has no external existence, so the accident would not exist. Thus it must need a particular substrate, and transfer is impossible. The passage then notes a consideration: the particular accident may only need a substrate without the condition of particularity. The commentary explains each phrase: the indeterminate cannot be a cause; taking particular existence externally leads to a circle; the objection about confined species is only relevant if such an accident exists; the need for a substrate is necessary; the answer about needing a non-particular substrate leads to alternation. The commentator also draws an analogy with matter and form: matter's individuation needs form as form, not as particular, and the individual unit must have a single cause. Further objections are addressed: the separate cause cannot be the cause of individuation because then the accident would be independent of the substrate, which is false. The passage continues with an objection that the same proof applies to bodies and place, to which the same answer is given. Finally, a sense perception objection is raised: smell and heat appear to transfer. The answer is that what occurs in the second substrate is a new similar individual, created by the free agent habitually or by the Active Intellect due to preparedness from contact. This entire passage systematically defends the doctrine that accidents do not transfer.

Key Terms
العرض
المحل
التعين
الحيز
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the body's change of place disproves the accident argument.
Correction: The passage notes that the body's relationship to place is different; place is not essential to the body's existence, whereas the accident's existence is essentially tied to its substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the main proof, why is it impossible for an accident to need a non-particular substrate?
A) Because a non-particular substrate does not have the power to sustain the accident.
B) Because a non-particular substrate does not exist in the external world.
C) Because the accident's particularity would then be unrelated.
D) Because the accident would then be independent.

Key Takeaway
An accident necessarily requires a particular substrate; a non-particular substrate is impossible, so transfer is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own existence is like an accident: entirely dependent on a particular substrate—your Creator. Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or do you constantly acknowledge that your very being is held in existence by Him at every moment? What one change would make that acknowledgment real in your daily life?
?
The proof shows that an accident cannot exist without its proper substrate. In your spiritual life, what ‘accidents’ (like good deeds or devotion) are you trying to maintain without their proper foundation in sincere faith? Are you relying on a non-particular ‘any place’ rather than the particular living relationship with Allah?
?
The opponent argued that a body changes place while remaining the same, seemingly contradicting the accident rule. Are there areas in your life where you try to justify a spiritual or ethical contradiction—thinking you can transfer your duties or virtues from one context to another without losing their essence? What particular ‘substrate’ have you abandoned?

Responding to Sense Perception: Smell and Heat
Response to the sense perception objection using creation of a new similar accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the impossibility of accident transfer against the empirical objection of smell and heat appearing to move.

Core Idea
When smell or heat seem to transfer, a new similar accident is created, not the same one moving.

A strong objection to the doctrine is that sense perception shows accidents like smell and heat moving from one object to another. The author responds by arguing that what appears in the second object is not the same accident, but a new one created by God habitually or by the Active Intellect due to the preparedness of the receiving object. This preserves the principle that accidents do not migrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the objection from common experience and the proposed answer.
The objection is based on everyday observations.
2
Identify the two explanatory models
The Ash'ari model: God creates a new accident by habit. The philosophers' model: Active Intellect emanates a new form due to preparedness.
Both models avoid transfer of the same accident.
3
Understand the key distinction
The accident in the second substrate is another individual, not the one from the first. So there is no actual transfer.
4
Reflect on the significance
This response shows that empirical evidence does not disprove the philosophical principle; it only seems to challenge it.

إلى محل معين لا من حيث أنه هذا المعين فيجوز الانتقال عليه وفيه أنه يلزم التوارد على سبيل البدل . الجواز . قوله : ( إذ قد يحتاج أي يجوز أن يحتاج لأنه اللازم من الدليل المذكور ولأنه مانع يكفيه يجوز مفارقته عنه كما في المادة بالنسبة إلى الصورة فإن تعينها إنما يحتاج إلى الصورة من حيث هي صورة ولذلك جاز مفارقتها من الصورة والجواب ما يشير إليه الشارح في تعريفات الهيولى من أن الواحد بالشخص لا بد أن The First Observatory - The Fifth Aim: That the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. If taken absolutely, it would not be a cause for a particular individuation; and if taken particularly, likewise, because the individuation of existences among individuals of a specific essence is only by the individuations of those individuals. If reversed, there would be a circle. Yes, an objection is raised against the proof: We do not accept that the relation of the separate cause to the whole is equal, because it is possible that it has a specific relation to a particular individuation, especially if the separate cause is a free agent, for it can choose what it wills. And it is also directed against it that it does not apply universally to an accident whose species is confined to its individual. (And it may be said) in proving the impossibility of transfer: The accident needs the substrate necessarily. So either the particular accident needs a particular substrate and does not leave it, because the particularity of that particular accident is connected to that particular substrate and necessitates it by its essence, or it needs a non-particular substrate which has no existence in the external world, because everything existing externally is particular in itself. Then it would follow that the accident does not exist externally due to the non-existence of the substrate it needs, and this is absolutely false. So the first is determined, and transfer is impossible, which is what is sought. And there is a consideration: Indeed, the particular accident may need a substrate without the condition of particularity. His saying: '(It would not be a cause for a particular individuation)' because the indeterminate cannot be an efficient cause for the particular. His saying: '(And if taken particularly, likewise, etc.)' means: If the particular external existence is taken, then that existence cannot exist externally, otherwise it would entail that the individuation of the accident is by what it inheres in, and we have refuted that, so it would be a mental consideration. So its individuation is only by the individuation of the substrate it subsists in; if it were a cause for its individuation, there would be a circle. His saying: '(And it is also directed against it, etc.)' This direction only applies if there exists an accident whose species is confined to its individual. His saying: '(The accident needs the substrate, otherwise it would not be an accident, and perhaps it is answered that it is permissible for it to need a particular substrate not insofar as it is this particular, so transfer would be permissible upon it, and therein it entails occurrence in alternation)' Possibility. His saying: '(Indeed, it may need)' means it may need, because it is necessitated by the aforementioned proof, and because it is a preventative that suffices for it to be possible for it to leave it, like in matter in relation to form, for its individuation only needs the form insofar as it is form, and therefore it is permissible for it to separate from the form. The answer is what the commentator points to in the definitions of prime matter: that the individual unit must have a single cause, so it is not rationally conceivable that the cause of the individuated accident is an absolute substrate. And the need of prime matter for form is for subsistence, not for individuation; rather, the opposite is true. Yes, then there is difficulty in what he mentioned in the discussion of individuation, as we indicated there. His saying: '(Because it is possible that it has a specific relation to a particular individuation)' It is said: It is not permissible for the separate cause to be the cause for the individuation of the accident, because then the accident would be self-sufficient in its individuation and existence without the subject, and one who is self-sufficient in both without the substrate does not need the substrate, so it would be independent of it, which is false. His saying: '(It does not apply universally to an accident whose species is confined to its individual)' because it is possible that its individuation is by itself and its essence or by its necessary concomitants. Individuation means: to an absolute substrate not restricted by individuation, and it is more general than the particular which is restricted by individuation. So that absolute taken without the condition of individuation exists in every particular of the particulars. Not to a substrate restricted by the condition of non-individuation, so that its existence externally is impossible, then it would follow that the accident does not exist in it. Rather we say: It needs the substrate absolute of individuation and does not need the one restricted to non-individuation, because from the non-consideration of individuation in the substrate that the particular accident needs, it does not necessitate the consideration of non-individuation in it, as you have known: that the absolute essence in which the existence of its accidents is not considered and not restricted by it is more general than the mixed essence restricted by it which exists externally, and the abstract essence restricted by its non-existence is impossible to exist externally. (And also, it, i.e. the proof you mentioned, applies to the body in relation to place): It is said: The body needs place in its being localized necessarily. So either it needs a particular place or a non-particular place, and the second is false because the non-particular has no existence, so it would follow that the localized body does not exist. So the first is determined, and transfer of the body from its particular place to another is not permissible. Thus your proof is refuted, and whatever your answer is, it is our answer. If it is said: This that you have mentioned regarding the impossibility of transfer for the accident is a denial of sense perception, for the smell of an apple transfers from it to what is adjacent to it, and heat transfers from fire to what contacts it, as sense perception witnesses. Then the answer: That which occurs in the second substrate, which is the adjacent or contacting, is another individual of smell or heat similar to the first occurring in the apple or fire, which the free agent brings about, according to us, by way of habit following adjacency or contact; or that other individual flows onto the second substrate from the Active Intellect, according to the philosophers, by way of necessity, due to a preparedness obtained from adjacency or contact.
Source pages p. 32

According to the Ash'ari explanation, how does smell appear in a nearby object?

?

?
If every experience of 'transfer' is actually God creating a new accident, then what does that imply about the constancy of His care for you? How would your day change if you consciously thanked Him for each sensation as a fresh gift?

Divine Attributes

The Second Verification: Divine Attributes Without Spatialization
Al-Jurjani presents the second verification that inherence is not spatial dependency, using God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that inherence of attributes does not imply spatialization, as demonstrated by God's attributes inhering in His essence without spatial dependency.

Core Idea
God's attributes inhere without spatialization — inherence is not spatial dependence.

Al-Jurjani now delivers a critical verification. He argues that the inherence of attributes does not require spatial location, because God's own attributes inhere in His essence without any spatialization. This refines the concept of qiyām and prevents a common objection. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist.
Notice the term 'shā'ibat al-taḥayyuz' — taint of spatialization.
2
Identify the key claim and objection
The passage states that the attributes of God inhere without any spatialization. It then preempts an objection that 'inherence of an accident means spatialization' by clarifying that this refers to created accidents, not God's attributes.
3
Understand the significance of 'shā'ibat al-taḥayyuz'
The phrase indicates that spatialization is not just absent but entirely irrelevant to divine attributes. They inhere in a non-spatial manner.
This distinguishes divine attributes from created accidents.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
This verification shows that inherence (qiyām) is a broader concept than spatial location. It applies to God's attributes without any spatial implications.
This refines the kalam understanding of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمر الثاني أوصاف الباري تعالى قائمة به كما سنبينه من غير شائبة تحيز في ذاته وصفاته 
قوله : (الأمر الثاني إلخ) يعني الحادث وليس فيه شائبة التحيز أصلاً وتحقيقاً ولا تقديراً فلا يرد أنَّ قيام العرض معناه التحيز لا مطلق القيام حتى يرد النقض بقيام صفاته تعالى بذاته .
The second [matter] is that the attributes of God Most High inhere in Him, as we will explain, without any taint of spatialization in His essence and attributes. His statement: '(The second matter, etc.)' means the created [accident], and there is in it no taint of spatialization at all, neither actual nor estimative. So it is not rebutted that the inherence of an accident means spatialization, not absolute inherence, such that the objection would be raised by the inherence of His attributes in His essence.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage provides the second verification. It is a simple but powerful argument: God's attributes inhere in His essence without any spatialization. Since God is not spatial, the inherence of His attributes cannot be spatial dependency. Therefore, inherence in general does not require spatialization. The commentator clarifies that this refers to created accidents (al-ḥādith) which have no spatialization whatsoever. This prevents the objection that 'inherence of an accident means spatialization' – because that would incorrectly exclude God's attributes. The argument shows that the meaning of inherence must be broader: it is the relation of an attribute to its subject, not necessarily a spatial one.

Key Terms
أوصاف الباري (attributes of God)
تحيز (taḥayyuz/spatialization)
حادث (ḥādith/created accident)
قيام (qiyām/inherence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inherence always implies a spatial relation.
Correction: The inherence of God's attributes shows that inherence does not require spatialization; it can be non-spatial.

Checkpoint
What does the second verification use to prove that inherence is not spatialization?
A) The impossibility of infinite regress
B) The attributes of God inhere without spatialization
C) The self-dependence of spatialization
D) The agreement of philosophers and theologians

Key Takeaway
Inherence of attributes does not entail spatialization; God's attributes inhere non-spatially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously imagine God's attributes as if they were located somewhere, like you imagine your own knowledge in your brain? What would it take to purify your mental image of God from all spatial assumptions starting today?
?
When you read that God's attributes inhere non-spatially, does that make you feel distant from Him or closer? How can you train your heart to find intimacy with a God who is not bound by space?
?
If someone asked you, 'Where is God's mercy?' you would say it is not in a place. But does your daily life—your prayers, your hopes—reflect a certainty that God's mercy is truly present with you without being located? What would change if you lived that conviction?

Roughness and Smoothness: Quality or Position?
Responds to an objection that roughness and smoothness are qualities existing on a surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author categorizes roughness and smoothness as positional accidents rather than qualities, and how this avoids the problem of accidents subsisting on accidents.

Core Idea
Roughness and smoothness are relational positions, not real qualities.

The philosophers argue that roughness and smoothness are qualities existing on a surface, which would imply accidents subsisting on accidents — a problematic chain. The author counters by reclassifying them under the category of position (al-waḍ‘), which involves relative conceptual relations. Even if one insists they are qualities, their subsistence on the body is mediated by the surface. This short passage demonstrates a key move in kalām ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the philosophers' argument is presented first, then the author's response.
The passage uses technical terms like 'kayf' (quality) and 'waḍ‘' (position).
2
Identify the philosophers' objection
The philosophers claim that roughness and smoothness are accidents from the category of quality, subsisting on a surface. Since the surface itself is an accident, this creates a problem: accidents subsisting on accidents.
This objection is a common challenge in discussions of divine attributes and ontology.
3
Understand the author's two-part response
The author gives two answers. First, he denies that they are qualities at all — they are from the category of position, which is relational and conceptual. Second, even if they were qualities, they subsist on the body not directly but through the surface.
The first response is the primary move; the second is a hypothetical concession.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why reclassifying roughness and smoothness as position solves the problem. Position is a relative accident that does not require the same kind of subsistence as quality.
This distinction preserves the coherence of the ontology without infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللحكماء احتجاج أنَّ الخشونة والملاسة عرضان من مقولة الكيف قائمان بالسطح لأنه الذي يوصف بهما والسطح عرض فأشار إلى جوابه بقوله ( وأما الخشونة والملاسة فإن سلم أنهما كيفيتان ) أي لا نسلم أنهما من باب الكيف بل هما من مقولة الوضع التي النسب الاعتبارية وإن سلم أنهما كيفيتان موجودتان (فقيامهما بالجسم من آخر وهو بالسطح
And the philosophers have an argument that roughness and smoothness are two accidents from the category of quality subsisting on the surface because it is that which is described by them, and the surface is an accident. So he pointed to his answer by his saying: (As for roughness and smoothness, if it is granted that they are two qualities) i.e., we do not grant that they are from the category of quality; rather they are from the category of position, which is relative conceptual matters. And if it is granted that they are two existing qualities, (then their subsistence on the body is from another [aspect] and that is on the surface).
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers that roughness and smoothness are qualities (كيف) existing on a surface, and since the surface is an accident, this would imply accidents subsisting on accidents. The author responds by denying that they are qualities; instead, they are from the category of position (وضع), which is relational and conceptual. Even if they were qualities, their subsistence on the body is mediated by the surface, not directly.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think roughness and smoothness are real qualities that inhere directly in a body.
Correction: They are actually relational positions (like 'being above') that are conceptually dependent, not real qualities.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the philosophers' argument that roughness and smoothness are qualities?
A) He agrees they are qualities but subsist directly on the body.
B) He denies they are from the category of quality and says they are from the category of position.
C) He argues that surfaces are not accidents.
D) He says roughness and smoothness do not exist at all.

Key Takeaway
Roughness and smoothness are from the category of position, not quality, resolving the objection of accidents subsisting on accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you judge others as having 'rough' or 'smooth' personalities without realizing these are not fixed qualities but relational perceptions? Can you recall a specific person you misjudged and re-evaluate that judgment now, acknowledging it may reflect your position relative to them rather than their essence?
?
The author reclassifies roughness and smoothness as positions to solve a theological problem. What is one belief about yourself that you have assumed is an unchangeable quality (e.g., 'I am impatient') but might actually be a relational position relative to circumstances or people? How would your self-perception shift if you treated it as changeable?
?
If roughness and smoothness are merely relative conceptual matters, then your sensory experiences are not absolute realities. What does this mean for your reliance on physical sensations to determine truth? Will you allow this lesson to weaken your attachment to sensory certainty, even if it feels unsettling?

The Argument from Impossibility of Two Similar Accidents and Its Response
Al-Jurjani presents the argument against endurance of accidents from impossibility of two similars and replies to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument against the endurance of accidents based on the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting, and the response that the second accident's existence is conditioned on the annihilation of the first.

Core Idea
The second similar accident is possible only after the first is annihilated.

This passage introduces a key argument in the debate over whether accidents endure. The opponent claims that if accidents endured, a second similar accident could not exist in the same substrate—yet such a thing is possible. The response clarifies that the second is only possible after the first is annihilated, and challenges the opponent with a parallel argument about substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Jurjani uses a very compressed style and includes marginal notes.
2
Identify the main argument
Locate the premise that if an accident endured, a similar accident could not exist in the same substrate. Then find the conclusion that endurance is false because a second similar accident is possible by consensus.
The argument relies on the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting.
3
Study the response (قلنا)
The response states that Allah creates the second accident by annihilating the first, so no coincidence occurs. Understand that the possibility of the second is conditional on removal of the first.
This refutes the opponent's claim that endurance makes the possible impossible.
4
Analyze the objections and replies
The text includes several objections (via قوله) that challenge the argument. Note the objection about the first moment, the witness of sensory change, and the general objection that would prevent any accident from existing.
Each objection is answered showing the argument does not prove its intended conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إجماعاً فلو بقي العرض في الحالة الثانية من وجوده لاستحال وجود مثله فيها وإلا اجتمع المثلان وذلك محال فبقاء الأعراض يوجب استحالة ما هو جائز اتفاقاً فيكون باطلاً . قلنا : يخلقه الله تعالى فيه أي في ذلك المحل بأن يعدم الأول عنه لأن جواز إيجاد مثله في محله في الحالة الثانية ليس مطلقاً بل هو مشروط بأعدام الأول ولا استحالة فيه كما لا استحالة في جواز إيجاد مثله في محله في الحالة الأولى على تقدير عدم إيجاد الأول فيها . وأيضاً ما ذكرتم يلزمكم في الجوهر لأنه يجوز خلق مثله في حيزه في الحالة الثانية من وجوده إجماعاً فلو كان باقياً لامتنع خلق مثله كذلك لاستحالة اجتماع المتحيزين بالذات في حيز واحد فانتقض دليلكم . قوله : ( كما لا استحالة إلخ ) إشارة إلى النقض بأنه لو تم لزم امتناع وجود العرض في الحالة الأولى لأن إيجاد مثله ممكن فيها فيلزم اجتماع المثلين فكما أن إيجاد مثله فيها مشروط بعدم إيجاد الأول كذلك إيجاد مثله في الحالة الثانية مشروط بإعدام الأول . قوله : واقع بلا اشتباه كالحركة بعد السكون وبالعكس والظلمة بعد الضوء وأمثالها لكن إجراؤه في كل عرض يدعي بقاءه ظاهراً إلا أن يدعي الحدس بان كل عرض يمكن زواله بواسطة إحساس الجزئيات الكثيرة . قوله : ( لو زال إلخ) أي لو جاز زواله لزم من فرض وقوعه محال لأنه لو زال فزواله حادث لذلك المتجدد بوقته انتفاء استعداد موضوعه له إلا في ذلك الوقت وهذا القدر يكفي في التخصيص فليفهم . قوله : ( كما لا استحالة إلخ ) إشارة إلى نقض إجمالي بأنه لو صح ما ذكر لزم امتناع وجود عرض في محل لأنَّ الله تعالى قادر على إيجاد مثله في ذلك المحل في ذلك الوقت فيلزم المحذور المذكور .
By consensus: if the accident remained in the second moment of its existence, the existence of its like in it would be impossible, otherwise the two similars would coincide, and that is impossible. So the endurance of accidents necessitates the impossibility of what is possible by consensus, so it is invalid. We say: Allah creates it in that substrate by annihilating the first of it, because the permissibility of creating its like in its substrate in the second moment is not absolute but is conditioned by the annihilation of the first, and there is no impossibility in that, just as there is no impossibility in the permissibility of creating its like in its substrate in the first moment on the assumption of not creating the first in it. Also what you mentioned applies to you with respect to the substance, because it is permissible to create its like in its locus in the second moment of its existence by consensus, so if it remained, creating its like would be likewise impossible due to the impossibility of two essentially extended entities in one locus. So your proof is invalidated. His saying: (as there is no impossibility etc.) is an indication of the objection that if it were valid, it would necessitate the impossibility of the existence of the accident in the first moment because creating its like in it is possible, thus the coincidence of two similars would follow; just as creating its like in it is conditioned by not creating the first, likewise creating its like in the second moment is conditioned by annihilating the first. His saying: occurring without ambiguity, like motion after rest and vice versa, darkness after light and the like, but applying it to every accident whose endurance is claimed is apparent unless one claims by intuition that every accident can be removed by means of sensing many particulars. His saying: (if it perishes etc.) means if its perishing is possible, then from the assumption of its occurrence a impossibility would follow, because if it perishes, its perishing is a new accident, and due to its renewal at its time, the preparedness of its subject for it ceases except at that time, and this much suffices for specification. His saying: (as there is no impossibility etc.) is an indication of a general objection that if what was mentioned were correct, it would necessitate the impossibility of the existence of an accident in a substrate because Allah is able to create its like in that substrate at that time, so the mentioned problem would follow.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the endurance of accidents, followed by the response and explanatory notes. The argument begins with a consensus premise: if an accident endured into a second moment, then a similar accident could not coexist in the same substrate—otherwise two identical things would coincide in one subject, which is impossible. Yet creating a similar accident in that substrate in the second moment is agreed upon as possible. Therefore, enduring accidents would make the possible impossible, so the premise that accidents endure is false. The response ( قلنا ) explains that when Allah creates a second similar accident, He first annihilates the first, so no coincidence occurs; the possibility of the second is conditional on the removal of the first. The response also argues that the same reasoning would apply to substances, thus invalidating the opponent's proof. The marginal notes (قوله) clarify the logic: the objection about the first moment is refuted by noting that creation of a like in the first moment is also conditional; the witness of sensory change (motion/rest, light/dark) supports the perishing of accidents, though not for every accident individually; and a general objection is raised that if the argument were sound, it would prevent any accident from existing at all, since Allah could create its like simultaneously.

Key Terms
العرض
اجتماع المثلين
الجوهر
المتحيزان بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument proves accidents cannot endure at all.
Correction: The response shows it only disproves endurance that prevents creation of a similar accident; endurance is possible if the first is annihilated.

Checkpoint
According to the response (قلنا), what must Allah do before creating a second similar accident in the same substrate?
A) Annihilate the first accident
B) Create the second accident independently of the first
C) Wait for the first accident to perish by itself
D) Change the substrate

Key Takeaway
The endurance of accidents is not disproven by the possibility of a second similar accident because the second is only possible after the first is annihilated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every accident in your life—every thought, every feeling, every moment of consciousness—is newly created by Allah and does not endure, then your current state is a fresh gift from Him. Do you truly live each moment as a unique, undeserved blessing, or do you take it for granted as if it were a continuation of the past?
?
The argument shows that the possibility of something new depends on the annihilation of the old. What in your life—habits, attachments, sins—needs to be 'annihilated' so that Allah can create something better in its place? Are you holding onto what must perish?
?
The opponent's logic seemed sound but had a blind spot: it failed to see that the condition for a second accident is the removal of the first. In your own understanding of Allah's attributes, where might you have blind spots that lead you to wrong conclusions about His power or justice? Will you actively seek to uncover those blind spots?

Accident Removal Is Not from an Agent
Al-Jurjani states that accident removal has no agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the removal of an accident cannot be attributed to any agent, based on the principle that non-existence is not an effect.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not an effect; thus no agent for accident removal.

This lesson presents a foundational argument in kalām: the passing away of an accident does not require an agent. The reasoning is simple yet profound—non-existence cannot be brought about because it is pure nothingness. By examining this claim, we see why theologians distinguish between existence and non-existence in causal attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the Arabic passage stating that an accident does not remain for two moments and that its removal has no agent. Note the crisp formulation.
The phrase 'ولا لفاعل أصلاً' is key.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that attributing removal to an agent would require the agent to produce non-existence, which is impossible because the effect of an agent is always something existent.
Recall the earlier discussion of 'effect of non-existence' vs. 'no effect'.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters for theology: if removal cannot be from an agent, then the persistence of accidents is a problem—they cannot last two moments on their own.
This sets up the next argument about removal by condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : أن العرض لا يبقي زمانين
٤٣
ولا لفاعل أصلاً ( أو نقول ) في الظال كون زوال للمختار ( ما أثره عدم فلا أثر له إذ لا فرق بين قولنا أثره لا وقولنا لا أثر له كما مر في بحث الإمكان ( فليس) الفاعل الذي أسند إليه زوال العرض (فاعلاً ) أصلاً سواء فرض حاراً أو موجناً ( وأما زواله بزوال شرط، فلأن ذلك الشرط إن كان عرضاً ) آخر ( تسلسل ) لأنَّا ننقل الكلام إلى العرض الذي هو الشرط فيكون زواله بزوال شرطه الذي هو عرض ثالث وهكذا فيلزم وجود أعراض غير متناهية بعضها شرط لبعض ( وإن كان ذلك الشرط (جوهراً والجوهر) في بقائه مشروط بالعرض لزم (الدور لأن بقاء كل واحد من الجوهر والعرض مشروط ببقاء الآخر موقوف عليه ) والاعتراض عليه ) أي على هذا الدليل الذي عده عمدة أنا نختار
قوله : ( ولا لفاعل أصلاً) إذ أثر الفاعل لا يكون لا شيئاً محضاً . قوله : ( فلأنَّ ذلك الشرط إن كان عرضاً إلخ انحصار الشرط في العرض والجوهر ممنوع لجواز أن يكون أمراً اعتبارياً.

فيلزم الاجتماع المستحيل مدفوع بأن وجوده في محل الباقي وزوال الباقي عنه في آن واحد وإن تقدم الأول على الثاني بالذات فلا محذور.
قوله : ( وإن كان جوهراً والجوهر مشروط بالعرض لزم الدور) قد أشرنا في أول المقصد إلى جواز أن يكون مثله من قبيل الشرط المتعاكس لا بد لنفيه من دليل على أن الدور لو سلم فإنما هو على تقدير كون الجوهر الشرط محل العرض الزائل فأما لو عمم فلا يظهر الدور وإنما لم ينقل الكلام إلى زوال الجوهر الشرط لأنه محسوس والتشكيك فيه سفسطة لا يعبأ به فإن قلت : قد يشاهد أنَّ العرض يزول محله فلا حاجة في إبطال كون زوال العرض بزوال شرطه الجوهر إلى إلزام الدور، قلت : ما ذكرته إبطال في بعض المواد والمدعى كلي بقي شيء وهو أن بقاء الجوهر عند المستدل مشروط بوجود العرض لا ببقائه على تقدير عدم بقاء الأعراض كما سلف منا في أول المقصد فعلى تقدير بقاء الأعراض ينبغي أن يثبت المستدل لزوم كون شرط بقاء الجوهر بقاء العرض لا نفس وجوده حتى يلزم الدور بزعمه إذ مجرد بقاء العرض لا يستلزم أن يكون نفسه شرط بقاء الجوهر كما لا يخفى .
The first observation post - the seventh objective: that an accident does not remain for two moments. 43. And neither is there any agent at all (or we say) in the removal [of the accident] for the chosen [view] (its effect is non-existence, so it has no effect, since there is no difference between our saying 'its effect is not' and our saying 'it has no effect', as passed in the discussion of possibility). So the agent to which the removal of the accident is attributed is not an agent at all, whether it is assumed to be hot or moving. As for its removal by the removal of a condition, then if that condition is another accident, then there is an infinite regress, because we transfer the discussion to the accident which is the condition, so its removal is by removal of its condition which is a third accident, and so on. Thus there follows the existence of infinite accidents, some of which are conditions of others. If that condition is a substance, and the substance in its persistence is conditioned by the accident, then there follows circularity, because the persistence of each of the substance and the accident is conditioned by the persistence of the other, dependent upon it. And the objection to it, i.e. to this argument which he considered the main one, is that we choose... His saying: (and neither is there any agent at all) for the effect of the agent is not pure nothingness. His saying: (for if that condition is an accident, etc.) the restriction of the condition to accident and substance is denied because it is possible that it be a mental consideration. His saying: (so there follows existence, etc.) i.e., at the time of removal of the accident, the existence of infinite accidents, which is impossible. His saying: (because the persistence of each of the substance, etc.) As for the persistence of the accident being conditioned by the substance, that is by the accident; as for the substance being conditioned by the persistence of the accident, that is because the existence of the substance is conditioned by the existence of the accident, like being in a place, for example, in every moment. So if you say that the [spatial] accidents are renewed, the desired result is established, namely the impossibility of persistence of the accident; and if you say they persist, then the persistence of the substance is conditioned by its persistence. And there is a discussion in it: first, because circularity would only follow if the accident which is conditioned is the very accident which is the condition, and likewise the substance which is the condition is the very substance which is conditioned, and neither of the two matters is necessary. Second, because we do not grant the establishment of the desired result on the assumption of renewal of the accident which is the condition for the persistence of the substance; what follows from it is only the impossibility of persistence of that accident. The claim that an impossible conjunction follows is refuted by [saying] that its existence in the place of the remaining [accident] and the removal of the remaining [accident] from it occur in a single moment, even if the first is prior to the second in essence, so there is no problem. His saying: (and if it is a substance, and the substance is conditioned by the accident, circularity follows) we have indicated at the beginning of the objective the possibility that such a case may be of the type of reciprocal condition, which requires a proof for its negation; as for the circularity, if it is granted, it is only on the assumption that the substance which is the condition is the place of the removed accident; but if it is generalized, the circularity does not appear. And the discussion is not transferred to the removal of the substance which is the condition because it is sensible and doubt about it is sophistry, not to be regarded. If you say: it may be observed that the accident is removed from its place, so there is no need in refuting that the removal of the accident is by removal of its condition the substance to require circularity; I say: what you have mentioned is a refutation in some particular cases, but the claim is universal. There remains a point: the persistence of the substance according to the arguer is conditioned by the existence of the accident, not by its persistence, on the assumption that accidents do not persist, as we have previously stated at the beginning of the objective. So on the assumption of persistence of accidents, the arguer must prove that the condition for the persistence of the substance is the persistence of the accident, not its existence itself, so that circularity follows according to his claim; for mere persistence of the accident does not entail that it itself is the condition for the persistence of the substance, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents a complex argument about the nature of accidents and their removal. The author (al-Jurjani) begins by stating the objective: to prove that an accident does not remain for two moments. The first argument is that the removal (zawal) of an accident cannot be attributed to an agent (fa'il) because the effect of an agent is not pure non-existence; attributing non-existence to an agent would be problematic. The author then considers two scenarios for removal: (1) removal by itself (which is rejected), and (2) removal by the removal of a condition. If the condition is another accident, infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, because the removal of the first accident would require the removal of a condition, which is another accident, and so on ad infinitum. If the condition is a substance, circularity (dawr) follows, because the persistence of the substance is conditioned by the persistence of the accident, while the persistence of the accident is conditioned by the persistence of the substance. The commentary then defends this argument against objections. It denies that the condition is restricted to accident and substance, allowing for a mental consideration. It also discusses the possibility of circularity being avoided if the substance and accident are not identical as condition and conditioned, and if the renewal of spatial accidents is assumed. The author refutes an objection that the claim of impossible conjunction (ijtima' mustahil) is refuted by affirming that the accident exists in its place while the remaining accident is removed in the same instant, though the first is prior in essence. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the persistence of the substance is conditioned by the existence of the accident, not its persistence, unless the accidents themselves persist, in which case the arguer must prove that the condition is the persistence of the accident to establish circularity.

Key Terms
عرض
زوال
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the removal of an accident, like the ceasing of heat, is caused by the opposite (cold) or by the substance itself.
Correction: The text shows that non-existence cannot be an effect; thus no agent can produce removal. The removal is simply the cessation of the accident without a cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the removal of an accident cannot be attributed to an agent?
A) Because the agent would have to produce non-existence, which is impossible.
B) Because accidents are eternal and never removed.
C) Because the agent is the substance itself.
D) Because removal is only by God's direct action.

Key Takeaway
Accident removal is not an effect of any agent because non-existence cannot be produced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If accident removal has no agent, then nothing 'causes' the end of a state. How does this reshape your understanding of why your good deeds or prayers may come to an end? What does that mean for your reliance on God?
?
This lesson shows that non-existence cannot be an effect. Reflect on a time when you felt like 'nothing was happening' in your life. Could it be that your assumption that something must be causing that emptiness is mistaken? How might this truth bring you peace or urgency?
?
If God does not directly create the removal of accidents (like the heat of a fire), then what is the basis for the causal connections you assume in your daily life? Are you attributing causality to things that have no real power? How does that change your gratitude and dependence?

Removal by Condition: Regress or Circularity
Al-Jurjani examines removal by condition, leading to regress or circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why removing an accident by removing its condition leads either to infinite regress (if the condition is another accident) or circularity (if the condition is a substance).

Core Idea
Removal by condition leads to regress or circularity, proving accidents cannot persist.

After establishing that accident removal has no agent, the author examines the alternative: removal by the removal of a condition. This leads to a dilemma. If the condition is another accident, we get an infinite chain of accidents. If the condition is a substance, then we get circularity because substance and accident depend on each other for persistence. Both are impossible, reinforcing the original claim that accidents cannot remain for two moments.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional argument
Read the passage explaining removal by condition. Notice the two cases: condition is accident or condition is substance.
The author uses 'إن كان عرضاً' and 'إن كان جوهراً'.
2
Follow the regress chain
If the condition is an accident, then its removal requires another condition, and so on infinitely. Understand why this is impossible.
The existence of an infinite chain of accidents at the moment of removal is impossible.
3
Understand the circularity
If the condition is a substance, then substance persistence depends on accident persistence, and accident persistence depends on substance persistence, creating a circular dependency.
This circularity is problematic because each requires the other.
4
Consider the objections and replies
The text includes objections and replies. Notice that the restriction to accident/substance is contested, and the possibility of reciprocal condition is raised.
This shows the depth of the debate.

The first observation post - the seventh objective: that an accident does not remain for two moments. 43. And neither is there any agent at all (or we say) in the removal [of the accident] for the chosen [view] (its effect is non-existence, so it has no effect, since there is no difference between our saying 'its effect is not' and our saying 'it has no effect', as passed in the discussion of possibility). So the agent to which the removal of the accident is attributed is not an agent at all, whether it is assumed to be hot or moving. As for its removal by the removal of a condition, then if that condition is another accident, then there is an infinite regress, because we transfer the discussion to the accident which is the condition, so its removal is by removal of its condition which is a third accident, and so on. Thus there follows the existence of infinite accidents, some of which are conditions of others. If that condition is a substance, and the substance in its persistence is conditioned by the accident, then there follows circularity, because the persistence of each of the substance and the accident is conditioned by the persistence of the other, dependent upon it. And the objection to it, i.e. to this argument which he considered the main one, is that we choose... His saying: (and neither is there any agent at all) for the effect of the agent is not pure nothingness. His saying: (for if that condition is an accident, etc.) the restriction of the condition to accident and substance is denied because it is possible that it be a mental consideration. His saying: (so there follows existence, etc.) i.e., at the time of removal of the accident, the existence of infinite accidents, which is impossible. His saying: (because the persistence of each of the substance, etc.) As for the persistence of the accident being conditioned by the substance, that is by the accident; as for the substance being conditioned by the persistence of the accident, that is because the existence of the substance is conditioned by the existence of the accident, like being in a place, for example, in every moment. So if you say that the [spatial] accidents are renewed, the desired result is established, namely the impossibility of persistence of the accident; and if you say they persist, then the persistence of the substance is conditioned by its persistence. And there is a discussion in it: first, because circularity would only follow if the accident which is conditioned is the very accident which is the condition, and likewise the substance which is the condition is the very substance which is conditioned, and neither of the two matters is necessary. Second, because we do not grant the establishment of the desired result on the assumption of renewal of the accident which is the condition for the persistence of the substance; what follows from it is only the impossibility of persistence of that accident. The claim that an impossible conjunction follows is refuted by [saying] that its existence in the place of the remaining [accident] and the removal of the remaining [accident] from it occur in a single moment, even if the first is prior to the second in essence, so there is no problem. His saying: (and if it is a substance, and the substance is conditioned by the accident, circularity follows) we have indicated at the beginning of the objective the possibility that such a case may be of the type of reciprocal condition, which requires a proof for its negation; as for the circularity, if it is granted, it is only on the assumption that the substance which is the condition is the place of the removed accident; but if it is generalized, the circularity does not appear. And the discussion is not transferred to the removal of the substance which is the condition because it is sensible and doubt about it is sophistry, not to be regarded. If you say: it may be observed that the accident is removed from its place, so there is no need in refuting that the removal of the accident is by removal of its condition the substance to require circularity; I say: what you have mentioned is a refutation in some particular cases, but the claim is universal. There remains a point: the persistence of the substance according to the arguer is conditioned by the existence of the accident, not by its persistence, on the assumption that accidents do not persist, as we have previously stated at the beginning of the objective. So on the assumption of persistence of accidents, the arguer must prove that the condition for the persistence of the substance is the persistence of the accident, not its existence itself, so that circularity follows according to his claim; for mere persistence of the accident does not entail that it itself is the condition for the persistence of the substance, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents a complex argument about the nature of accidents and their removal. The author (al-Jurjani) begins by stating the objective: to prove that an accident does not remain for two moments. The first argument is that the removal (zawal) of an accident cannot be attributed to an agent (fa'il) because the effect of an agent is not pure non-existence; attributing non-existence to an agent would be problematic. The author then considers two scenarios for removal: (1) removal by itself (which is rejected), and (2) removal by the removal of a condition. If the condition is another accident, infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, because the removal of the first accident would require the removal of a condition, which is another accident, and so on ad infinitum. If the condition is a substance, circularity (dawr) follows, because the persistence of the substance is conditioned by the persistence of the accident, while the persistence of the accident is conditioned by the persistence of the substance. The commentary then defends this argument against objections. It denies that the condition is restricted to accident and substance, allowing for a mental consideration. It also discusses the possibility of circularity being avoided if the substance and accident are not identical as condition and conditioned, and if the renewal of spatial accidents is assumed. The author refutes an objection that the claim of impossible conjunction (ijtima' mustahil) is refuted by affirming that the accident exists in its place while the remaining accident is removed in the same instant, though the first is prior in essence. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the persistence of the substance is conditioned by the existence of the accident, not its persistence, unless the accidents themselves persist, in which case the arguer must prove that the condition is the persistence of the accident to establish circularity.

تسلسل

بقاء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that removal by condition is a viable explanation for accident removal, avoiding the need to accept that accidents do not persist.
Correction: The author shows that any conditional explanation leads to an impossible regress or circularity, thus reinforcing the original claim that accidents cannot persist for two moments.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if the condition for an accident's persistence is another accident, what follows?
A) Circularity
B) Infinite regress
C) Both regress and circularity
D) Nothing impossible

Key Takeaway
Removal by condition leads to either infinite regress (if condition is accident) or circularity (if condition is substance), both impossible, supporting the view that accidents do not persist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that accidents do not persist but are renewed each moment. If even your location and existence are accidents constantly recreated by Allah, how does that change the way you perceive your own stability? Are you living as though you have a fixed, independent existence, when really you are dependent on Him at every instant? What would it mean to truly internalise that you are never the same from one moment to the next without His continuous act of creation?
?
The argument against removal by condition uses the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity. Reflect on your own life: are there areas where you depend on an infinite chain of excuses or justifications (e.g., 'I'll repent later, then later, then...')? Or are there circular dependencies in your priorities (e.g., 'I seek knowledge to act, but I act only after I know') that prevent real change? Can you, right now, identify one such pattern and commit to breaking it by relying on Allah's direct intervention?
?
Al-Jurjani notes that the condition could be a mental consideration (amr i'tibari). This shows that even the most rigorous arguments must humbly accept their limits when faced with the unseen. In your own beliefs about Allah, have you ever assumed that your reasoning exhausts all possibilities? Are you ready to accept that His wisdom may transcend your logical categories, and that some of His attributes (like His will) cannot be fully captured by human conditionality? How does this humility affect your worship and trust in Him?
