Ibn Sina on Whiteness: No Sophistry Here
Defense of Ibn Sina against sophistry charge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina's position that whiteness is not light does not entail sophistry, and how the commentator refutes al-Razi's misreading.

Core Idea
Whiteness can be light or a distinct color; admitting one does not deny the other.

This passage defends Ibn Sina against a serious charge: that his view on whiteness leads to sophistry—distrust of the senses. The commentator carefully shows that Ibn Sina never denied the reality of perceived whiteness; he only distinguished between whiteness as light and whiteness as a genuine color. This lesson helps you see how to read philosophical arguments with precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the objection-and-answer structure: first a quote from Ibn Sina, then an accusation, then a resolution.
Ibn Sina’s words are quoted directly; the commentator builds his defense on them.
2
Identify the accusation of sophistry
Find where the commentator says that Ibn Sina's view would lead to 'sophistry and loss of trust in sensation.' Understand why someone might think that.
The accusation is that if whiteness is just light, then there is no real whiteness in foam—only an illusion.
3
See how the commentator resolves it
The commentator explains that Ibn Sina explicitly says the perceived whiteness in foam is real reflected light, and he left open the possibility of other kinds of whiteness. No sophistry follows.
Also note the criticism of al-Razi for distorting Ibn Sina’s words to make objections easier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا فقد بان بهذه الوجوه أن البياض بالحقيقة في الأشياء ليس بضوء ثم لسنا نمنع أن يكون للضوء المضيء تأثير في التبيض . قال المصنف : ( وإذ قد تقرر ذلك فإنه قد اعترف ) أي ابن سينا بأن لا بياض فيما ذكروه من (الأمثلة وهي زبد الماء وأخواته ويلزم السفسطة ) وارتفاع الأمان عن الحس بالكلية. وهاهنا بحث وهو أنه قد صرح فيما نقلناه كلامه بأن المحسوس في هذه الأمثلة أمر موجود هو الضوء المتعاكس وجعله بياضاً حادثاً بطريق مخصوص وقال : وأما أنه هل يكون بياض غير هذا فمما لم أعلم بعد امتناعه ووجوده وسيأتي لي كلام في هذا المعنى أشد استقصاء وأشار به إلى الوجوه الخمسة الدالة على أنّ البياض قد يحدث بطريق آخر فيظهر أن البياض لون مغاير للضوء المسمى في تلك الأمثلة بياضاً وليس في هذا سفسطة وارتفاع أمان، لكن الإمام الرازي كما هو دأبه يتصرف فيما ينقله عنه ليتسع له مجال الاعتراض عليه ويقلده في ذلك من يتبعه .
Ibn Sina said: 'It has become clear by these considerations that whiteness in reality in things is not light, then we do not deny that bright light has an effect in whitening.' The author (al-musannif) said: 'Since that has been established, he (Ibn Sina) has admitted that there is no whiteness in the examples they mentioned, namely foam of water and its likes, and sophistry follows and the loss of trust in sensation entirely.' And here is a research: it is that he stated clearly in what we quoted of his speech that the perceived thing in these examples is an existing thing, which is reflected light, and he made it a whiteness occurring in a particular way, and he said: 'As for whether there is whiteness other than this, I have not yet known its impossibility or existence, and there will come from me a speech on this meaning with greater investigation.' And he pointed thereby to the five considerations indicating that whiteness may occur in another way, so it appears that whiteness is a color different from the light called whiteness in those examples, and there is no sophistry or loss of trust in this. But Imam al-Razi, as is his habit, manipulates what he transmits from him to make room for objection against him, and those who follow him imitate him in that.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. It begins by quoting Ibn Sina's conclusion that whiteness is not light, though light can contribute to whitening. The commentator (al-musannif) then raises an objection: Ibn Sina's statement seems to admit there is no real whiteness in examples like foam—which would lead to sophistry and distrust of sense perception. But the commentator immediately provides a research (bahth) that resolves the objection. He explains that in the quoted text, Ibn Sina explicitly stated that what is perceived in those examples is a real entity—reflected light—which he calls a whiteness that occurs in a specific manner. Ibn Sina also left open the possibility of another kind of whiteness, promising further discussion. Thus, the whiteness in those examples is a light that has become whiteness in a particular way, not a denial of whiteness altogether. The commentator then criticizes al-Razi for distorting Ibn Sina's words to make room for objections, and warns that followers of al-Razi imitate this practice.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Ibn Sina considered the perceived whiteness in foam to be real (reflected light) and allowed for other kinds of whiteness, so no denial of sense perception occurs.

Checkpoint

B) Because Ibn Sina considered the perceived whiteness in foam to be a real entity (reflected light) and allowed for other kinds of whiteness.
C) Because Ibn Sina claimed that all whiteness is merely light.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's view that whiteness is not light does not deny the reality of perceived whiteness; careful reading shows he recognized reflected light as whiteness while allowing for other forms.

?

?
This lesson exposes how a misreading can lead to false accusation. Is there a person you have misunderstood because you only heard part of their statement? Will you go back and listen to their full words today, even if it means admitting you were wrong?

Al-Razi's View: Light as Condition for Vision, Not Existence
Al-Razi argues that light is a condition for seeing color, not for its existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi holds that light is a condition for seeing color but not for its existence, and how this differs from the view that darkness causes color to cease.

Core Idea
Light enables vision; color exists even in darkness.

Al-Razi presents a nuanced position on the role of light in perception. While it is common to think that darkness makes color disappear, al-Razi argues that color persists; it is only our ability to see it that is absent. This passage clarifies the distinction between the conditions for existence and the conditions for perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Pay attention to the key claim: light is a condition for vision, not for the existence of color.
Notice that al-Razi distinguishes between the absence of vision and the absence of the object itself.
2
Identify the main argument
The passage states that we are certain about the absence of vision in darkness, but not about the absence of color itself. This leads to the conclusion that light is a condition for vision, not existence.
The key phrase: 'فإنَّ رؤيته زائدة على ذاته' (vision is additional to its essence).
3
Analyze the cave example
Consider the example of a person in a cave. They cannot see someone outside because the light does not surround the visible object. When a fire is lit, the outside person becomes visible, showing that vision depends on light reaching the object.
This example illustrates that the obstacle is not the darkness itself but the absence of light as a condition.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Think about why this distinction matters. It separates the ontological existence of color from its epistemological perception. This has implications for discussions of divine attributes and causality.
Al-Razi's view preserves the reality of color independent of observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشهور فيما بين الجمهور وهو مختار الإمام الرازي أنه أي الضوء شرط لرؤيته لا لوجوده فإنَّ رؤيته زائدة على ذاته والمتحقق المتيقن عدم رؤيته في الظلمة وأما عدمه في نفسه فلا فانتفاء الرؤية في الظلمة لعدم شرط الرؤية لا لوجود العائق عنها ولا لعدم اللون في نفسه والجالس في الغار إنما لا يراه الخارج عنه لعدم إحاطة الضوء المحيط بالمرئي ولذلك يرى الجالس الخارج المستضيء بالنار.
The well-known view among the common people, and it is the chosen view of Imam al-Razi, is that light is a condition for its vision, not for its existence. For its vision is additional to its essence, and the certain verified thing is the absence of its vision in darkness. As for its non-existence in itself, no. So the negation of vision in darkness is due to the absence of the condition of vision, not due to the existence of an obstacle, nor due to the non-existence of color in itself. The person sitting in the cave does not see the one outside him because of the lack of light surrounding the visible object; therefore, the sitter sees the outside person illuminated by the fire.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi's position, which is the popular view, that light is a condition for the perception of color, not for its ontological existence. The argument is that we are certain that color is not seen in darkness, but we are not certain that color does not exist in itself. Therefore, the lack of sight is explained by the absence of the condition for vision (light), not by the absence of color nor by an obstacle (since the dark air is not an obstacle). The cave example is reinterpreted: the person in the cave does not see the outside person because the outside person is not illuminated (the light does not surround the visible), but when the fire is lit, the outside person becomes visible, confirming that vision depends on light reaching the object.

Key Terms

الرؤية
الوجود
الظلمة
الضوء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a color is not seen in darkness, it must not exist in darkness.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that absence of vision does not imply absence of existence; darkness removes the condition for seeing, not the color itself.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why is color not seen in darkness?
A) Because color does not exist in darkness.
B) Because the dark air is an obstacle.
C) Because the condition for vision (light) is absent.
D) Because the eye is not capable.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi teaches that light is a condition for seeing color, not for its existence; color remains even in darkness, and its non-observation is due to the absence of light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that color exists even in darkness; yet do you live as if only what you can see is real? What in your life have you denied simply because it is not currently 'illuminated' to you? Commit today to acknowledging one unseen truth that you have been ignoring.
?
Al-Razi insists we must not conflate the absence of perception with absence of existence. Think of something you stopped believing in because you no longer 'see' evidence for it. Are you sure it is not there, or are you just not perceiving it? What would it take to seek the light of that matter? Write down one step you can take this week.
?
If you treat the lack of visible results as proof that something does not exist, are you repeating the error this passage corrects? In what area of your faith or relationships are you making that mistake? Resolve to change one assumption today based on this lesson.

Refuting Sound as a Body and the Harmony of the Spheres
Al-Jurjani refutes that sound is a body and discusses the philosophical view of celestial sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes the claim that sound is a body and why the Ash'ari view affirms sound as a direct creation of God without physical causes.

Core Idea
Sound is not a body; it is directly created by Allah without physical causes.

This passage tackles two interconnected ideas: the claim that sound is a body, and the ancient philosophical notion of celestial harmonies. Al-Jurjani uses rational arguments (tangibility, the need for air) to refute these, then presents the Ash'ari occasionalist truth that sound is created directly by God. We'll follow his step-by-step reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the rhythm of al-Jurjani's argument come through.
Notice the phrase 'وبهذا الدليل أيضاً' linking back to a previous argument.
2
Identify the argument against sound as a body
Find the two pieces of evidence al-Jurjani uses to refute that sound is a body: (1) the previous argument, (2) the tangibility of bodies vs. sound.
He says bodies are tangible 'ولو ثانياً' (even secondarily), unlike sound.
3
Understand the objection about celestial spheres
Al-Jurjani presents a possible objection: if sound requires air, then the spheres (which have no air) would produce no sound, contradicting Pythagorean claims. Consider how this strengthens his refutation.
The objection is actually from the philosophers who believed in celestial music.
4
Grasp the Ash'ari occasionalist position
Al-Jurjani states the truth: sound occurs by the mere creation of God, not through physical processes like waving air or striking. This is the key theological point.
This aligns with Ash'ari occasionalism—all events are directly caused by God.
5
Reflect on the methodological note
Notice the concluding remark: false opinions are often mentioned without explicit refutation unless necessary. Ask yourself why al-Jurjani includes this observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا الدليل أيضاً يبطل ما قيل من أن الصوت جسم وكذا بدليل ملموسية الجسم ولو ثانياً دون الصوت. قوله : ( وسببه القريب تموج (الهواء قيل : إن كان حدوث الصوت وسماعه مشروطين بالهواء لم يكن لتماس الأفلاك صوت ولو فرض لم يكن وصوله إلينا لامتناع النفوذ من جرم لكن ينسب إلى الأساطين من القدماء أنهم يثبتون للأفلاك أصواتاً عجيبة ونغمات غريبة يتحير من سماعها العقل ويتعجب منها النفس وحكي عن فيثاغورس أنه عرج بنفسه إلى العالم العلوي فسمع بصفاء جوهر نفسه وذكاء قلبه نغمات الأفلاك وأصوات حركاتها ثم رجع إلى استعمال القوى البدنية ورتب عليها الأركان والنغمات وكمل علم الموسيقى والحق عندنا أن الصوت يحدث بمحض خلق الله تعالى من غير تأثير لتموج الهواء والقرع والقلع كسائر الحوادث وكثيراً ما تورد الآراء الباطلة للفلاسفة من غير تعرض لبيان البطلان إلا فيما يحتاج إلى زيادة بيان
And by this evidence also is invalidated what was said that sound is a body, and likewise by the evidence of the tangibility of a body even secondarily, unlike sound. His statement: (And its proximate cause is the waving of air). It is said: If the occurrence of sound and its hearing are conditioned by air, then there would be no sound from the contact of the spheres, and even if it were supposed, it would not reach us due to the impossibility of penetration through a body. But it is attributed to the masters of the ancients that they affirm for the spheres wondrous sounds and strange melodies that the intellect is bewildered by hearing them and the soul marvels at them. And it is reported from Pythagoras that he ascended in his soul to the celestial world and heard, by the purity of his essence and the intelligence of his heart, the melodies of the spheres and the sounds of their motions. Then he returned to the use of bodily powers and arranged upon them the elements and melodies and completed the science of music. And the truth with us is that sound occurs by the mere creation of God Most High, without any effect from the waving of air, strike, or tear, like all other events. And often false opinions of the philosophers are mentioned without being refuted, except where additional clarification is needed.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and a statement of the Ash'ari position. Al-Jurjani first uses the previous argument (that sound is not visible) to refute the claim that sound is a body, because bodies are tangible (even secondarily) and sound is not. He then addresses a possible objection: if sound requires air, then the celestial spheres, which are in contact without air, would produce no sound, contradicting the ancient philosophers (like Pythagoras) who claimed to hear the harmony of the spheres. Al-Jurjani rejects this philosophical fancy and declares the truth from the Ash'ari perspective: sound is created directly by God, without intermediate physical causes like waving, striking, or tearing. He concludes by noting that such false philosophical views are often cited without explicit refutation unless necessary. This underscores the Ash'ari occasionalist stance.

Key Terms
جسم
ملموسية
فلك
فيثاغورس
خلق
موسيقى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sound must have a physical cause like air waves or striking bodies to occur.
Correction: Al-Jurjani affirms that sound occurs by the mere creation of God, without any effect from physical causes.

Checkpoint
According to the 'truth' mentioned by al-Jurjani, how does sound occur?
A) By the waving of air.
B) By the strike of bodies.
C) By the mere creation of God.
D) By the motion of the spheres.

Key Takeaway
Sound is not a body and its occurrence is directly from Allah, not from physical interactions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah creates every sound you hear—every word, every rustle—or do you functionally attribute them to physical causes? How would your awareness of His direct creation change your attentiveness to what you hear?
?

?
When you listen to music or any sound, do you ever pause to thank the One who created that sound in that moment? If not, will you commit to a daily practice of recognizing Allah's immediate creation in the sounds around you, starting today?

Life: A Distinct Power from Equilibrium
Definition of life and distinction from other faculties via Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic philosophy defines life as a distinct power emanating from a qualitative equilibrium, separate from faculties like sensation, movement, and nutrition.

Core Idea
Life is not its functions—it is their source.

This passage from a commentary on al-Mawaqif tackles a fundamental question: what is life? The author, building on Ibn Sina's insight, defines life as a power arising from a specific qualitative equilibrium in the body, from which all other animal faculties emanate. Before we read, consider: why might someone think life is just the sum of its functions? The passage addresses this misconception directly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Note the structure: definition, summary, warning, citation.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the definition of life
Find where the author defines life. What is life dependent on? What flows from it?
Look for 'تعريف الحياة بالاعتدال النوعي'.
3
Understand the common mistake
The passage mentions a mistaken view about life. What is it? How does Ibn Sina refute it?
The mistake: thinking life is identical to sensation, movement, nutrition.
4
Reflect on the relationship
Based on the passage, is life the same as its faculties or something else? Why does the author emphasize this distinction?
Life is a prerequisite and source, not identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول - المقصد الأول : تعريف الحياة بالاعتدال النوعي ويفيض منها سائر القوى الحيوانية كقوى الحس والحركة والتصرف في الأغذية. وتلخيصه أنه إذا حصل في مركب عنصري اعتدال نوعي يليق بنوع حيواني فاض عليه من المبدأ قوة الحياة ثم انبعثت منها قوى أخرى، أعني الحواس الباطنة والظاهرة والقوى المحركة إلى جلب المنافع ودفع المضار. كل ذلك بتقدير العزيز العليم فالحياة تابعة للاعتدال المذكور ومتبوعة لما عداها من القوى الموجودة في الحيوان. وقد يتوهم أنَّ الحياة هي قوة الحس والحركة الإرادية وقوة التغذية بعينها لا أنها قوة أخرى مستتبعة لهذه القوى كما ذكرنا. فلذلك قال ابن سينا في كليات القانون دفعاً لهذا التوهم إنها أي الحياة غير قوة الحس والحركة وغير قوة التغذية والتنمية. ويدل عليه أي على التغاير قوله القوى الحيوانية أي الموجودة في الحيوان كما يدل عليه آخر كلامه وليس المراد ما يقابل النفسانية والطبيعية فإنها بهذا المعنى نفس الحياة.
The first type - first aim: definition of life by qualitative equilibrium, and from it flow all the animal faculties such as the powers of sensation, movement, and managing nutrition. Its summary: when there occurs in an elemental composite a qualitative equilibrium befitting a type of animal, there flows onto it from the principle a power of life, then from it emanate other powers, namely the inner and outer senses and the motive powers for attracting benefits and repelling harms. All that by the decree of the Mighty, the Knowing. So life is dependent on the mentioned equilibrium and is a source for all other powers present in the animal. And it might be mistakenly thought that life is exactly the power of sensation, voluntary movement, and the power of nutrition, not that it is another power entailing these powers as we mentioned. Therefore Ibn Sina said in the Colleagues of the Canon, repelling this misunderstanding: 'It (i.e., life) is not the power of sensation and movement nor the power of nutrition and growth.' And it indicates, i.e., the difference, his saying 'the animal faculties' i.e., present in the animal, as indicated by the end of his speech; and what is meant is not what is opposed to the psychical and natural, for then it would be life itself.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a definition of life as a power that arises from a specific qualitative equilibrium in an elemental composite. The commentator explains that from this life-power emanate all other animal faculties, such as sensation, movement, and nutrition. He then warns against a common misunderstanding: some think that life is identical to these powers themselves. To refute this, he cites Ibn Sina's statement from the Canon of Medicine that life is not the power of sensation, movement, or nutrition. The commentator further clarifies that when Ibn Sina speaks of 'animal faculties', he means the faculties found in animals, not the psychical or natural faculties that would make life identical to them. Thus, life is a distinct power that underlies and enables the other faculties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Life is identical to the powers of sensation, movement, and nutrition.
Correction: Life is a distinct power from which these faculties emanate; it is not the same as them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between life and the other animal faculties?
A) Life is identical to the powers of sensation, movement, and nutrition.
B) Life is a distinct power from which other faculties emanate.

D) Life is the same as the qualitative equilibrium.

Life is a distinct power arising from a qualitative equilibrium, not to be conflated with the faculties it enables.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that life is a distinct power, not just the sum of your actions. When you look at your daily routines—eating, moving, sensing—do you ever pause to recognize that your very capacity to do these is a gift from the Creator, separate from the actions themselves? What would change if you started each day with awareness of this life-power as a direct bestowal, not a given?
?
This passage teaches that life emanates from a 'principle' (المبدأ) by divine decree (بتقدير العزيز العليم). If your life is not self-generated but continually received from Allah, how does that reshape your understanding of your own ownership over your existence? What would you do differently today if you truly felt your life as a loan from the Almighty?
?
Ibn Sina and the author warn against conflating life with its functions. Have you ever reduced your life to what you do—your productivity, your actions—rather than seeing your 'being alive' as a deeper reality? How would you live today if you prioritized your life as a trust from Allah over the mere functions you perform?

Proof that Life is Distinct from Psychical Powers: The Paralyzed Limb Argument
Ibn Sina's argument distinguishing life from psychical powers via the paralyzed limb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that life is a distinct power from sensation and movement, as demonstrated by the paralyzed limb's preservation from decay.

Core Idea
The living paralyzed limb proves life is not the same as sensation or movement.

This lesson presents Ibn Sina's ingenious medical reasoning to distinguish life from the psychical powers. Using the example of a paralyzed limb that remains alive yet lacks sensation and voluntary movement, he shows that life must be a separate power. The passage also outlines the physiological process of spirit generation and the preparatory power required for psychical powers to arise.

Guided Path
1
Understand the physiological background
The passage begins with a summary of how spirit is generated and how a preparatory power must precede psychical powers. Read this carefully to set the context.
This background comes from Ibn Sina's Canon.
2
Examine the paralyzed limb example
The core evidence: a paralyzed limb lacks sensation and movement but remains alive (preserved from decay). Contrast it with a dead limb.
Notice that the numb limb also shows the same pattern.
3
Identify the inference
Ibn Sina infers that life is different from the powers of sensation and movement because different effects (life vs. lack of psychical powers) indicate different causes.
This is the standard medical method of distinguishing powers.
4
Confirm the conclusion
The passage explicitly concludes that life is distinct from the psychical powers, which are the perceptive and motive powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتلخيصه إلخ) لا يخفى ما فيه من الإجمال والتفصيل ما في القانون أنه كما يتولد تكاثف الأخلاط بحسب مزاج ما جوهر كثيف هو العضو أو جزء من العضو فقد يتولد من بخارية الأخلاط ولطافتها جوهر لطيف هو الروح. وكما أن الكبد معدن الأول، كذلك القلب معدن الثاني وهذا الروح إذا حدث على مزاجه الذي ينبغي أن يكون له استعد لقبول قوة هي التي تعد الأعضاء كلها لقبول القوى الأخرى النفسانية وغيرها. والقوى النفسانية لا تحدث في الروح والأعضاء إلا بعد حدوث هذه القوة. ألا ترى أن العضو المفلوج والعضو الخدر فاقد في الحال لقوة الحس والحركة لمزاج فيه يمنعه من قبولهما أو سدة عارضة بين الدماغ وبينه في الأعصاب المنبعثة إليه ذلك وهو مع والعضو الذي يعرض له الموت فاقد للحس والحركة ويعرض له أن ينقض ويفسد، فإذا في العضو المفلوج قوة تحفظ حياته حتى إذا زال المانع فاضت عليه قوة الحس والحركة وكان مستعداً لثبوتهما بسبب صحة القوة الحيوانية، وإنما المانع هو الذي يمنعه عن قبولهما بالفعل، ولا كذلك العضو الميت انتهى. ولا خفاء في أن مقصوده بيان مغايرتها لقوة الحس والحركة من حيث يصدر عنهما الحس والحركة بالفعل، والاستدلال باختلاف الآثار على اختلاف القوى كما هو شأن الطبيب. ألا ترى أن العضو المفلوج توجد فيه الحياة وهي الحافظة للأجزاء عن التعفن والتفرق والبلى، وليس له قوة الحس والحركة، وكذا الحال في العضو الخدر، فإنه أيضاً فاقد في الحال قوة الحس والحركة، مع وجود قوة الحياة فيه. فظهر أن الحياة مغايرة للقوى النفسانية التي هي القوى المدركة والمحركة.
His saying: 'And its summary, etc.' It is not hidden what it contains of conciseness and detail in the Canon: that just as from the condensation of humors according to a certain temperament a dense substance—the organ or part of the organ—is generated, so from the vaporiness and subtlety of humors a subtle substance called the spirit is generated. And just as the liver is the mine of the first, so the heart is the mine of the second. And this spirit, when it occurs in its proper temperament, becomes prepared to receive a power which prepares all the organs to receive the other psychical and other powers. And the psychical powers do not occur in the spirit and organs except after the occurrence of this power. Do you not see that the paralyzed limb and the numb limb are at the moment devoid of the power of sensation and movement due to a temperament that prevents them from receiving them or an obstruction between the brain and it in the nerves emanating to it? And the limb that is struck by death is devoid of sensation and movement and is subject to putrefaction and decay. So in the paralyzed limb there is a power that preserves its life, so that when the obstacle is removed, the power of sensation and movement flows onto it, and it is prepared for their stability due to the soundness of the animal power. The obstacle is only what prevents it from receiving them in actuality, unlike the dead limb. There is no doubt that his intention is to show its difference from the power of sensation and movement in terms of producing sensation and movement in actuality, and the inference by difference of effects on difference of powers, as is the way of the physician. Do you not see that the paralyzed limb contains life, which preserves the parts from putrefaction, decay, and wearing away, while it has no power of sensation and movement? Likewise the numb limb: it also is at the moment devoid of the power of sensation and movement while having the power of life in it. So it becomes clear that life is different from the psychical powers, which are the perceptive and motive powers.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is an argument from Ibn Sina to prove that life is distinct from the psychical powers (sensation and voluntary movement). The commentator begins by summarizing the Canon's account of how a subtle spirit is generated from humors, and how a preparatory power must precede the psychical powers. He then presents the key evidence: a paralyzed or numb limb lacks sensation and movement (psychical powers) yet remains alive; it does not decay like a dead limb. The preservation of the limb from putrefaction shows that life is present. When the obstacle is removed, sensation and movement return, proving that life is a distinct power that persists even when psychical powers are absent. The commentator emphasizes that Ibn Sina uses the medical method of inferring distinct powers from distinct effects. The conclusion is explicit: life is different from the psychical powers (the perceptive and motive powers).

Misconception: A paralyzed limb is dead because it cannot move or feel.
Correction: The paralyzed limb remains alive; it preserves from decay and can regain sensation when the obstacle is removed.

What does the case of the paralyzed limb demonstrate about life?
A) Life is the same as the power of sensation and movement.
B) Life can exist without the power of sensation and movement.
C) The paralyzed limb is dead because it cannot move.
D) Life is destroyed when sensation is absent.

Key Takeaway
Life is a distinct power that preserves the body from decay, and it can exist independently of sensation and movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a limb that is paralyzed or numb? No. But what about your heart? Is your heart paralyzed to the call of truth, numb to the signs of Allah? And yet you still have life—a life that preserves your body. Will you continue to let your heart remain numb, or will you seek to remove the obstacle so that spiritual sensation and movement can flow?
?
The passage teaches that life is a power that preserves from decay. Your physical life will end one day. But your spiritual life—your īmān—does it preserve you from spiritual decay? When was the last time you felt your faith actively preserving your heart from putrefying with heedlessness?
?
Ibn Sina uses the example of a paralyzed limb to show that life and psychical powers are distinct. Reflect on your own life: Are there areas where you have 'life'—you exist, you breathe—but you have no spiritual 'sensation' or 'movement'? You pray but your heart does not feel; you hear the Qurʾān but it does not move you. What is the obstacle? And what will you do today to remove it?

Proof of Life's Distinction from Nutritive Power and Classification of Powers
The commentator proves life is distinct from nutritive power using the withered limb and plants, then classifies three genera of powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that distinguishes life from nutritive power and how the three genera of powers in animals are classified.

Core Idea
Life and nutrition are distinct; each can exist without the other.

In this passage, the commentator presents a powerful argument from the withered limb to show that life is not the same as the natural nutritive power. He then turns to classify the three genera of powers found in animals—psychical, natural, and a third—though the text is fragmented. The discussion also addresses objections, strengthening the overall proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the main argument
Look for the key examples: a withered limb retains life but lacks nutrition, while plants have nutrition without life. Notice how these examples prove the distinction.
The withered limb does not decay, showing it is alive despite no nutrition.
3
Understand the classification
Find the three genera of powers: psychical (senses and motor), natural (nutrition, growth, generation), and a third (conditions/obstacles). Note that the text cuts off during the classification.
The classification is incomplete due to fragmented text.
4
Consider the objections and replies
The passage includes objections, e.g., that the withered limb might still have nutrition, and the commentator's responses. See how these strengthen the original argument.
Objections help clarify the reasoning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مغايرتها للقوى الطبيعية التي تتصرف في الأغذية فيدل عليها قوله : ( وتوجد ) أي الحياة (في) العضو ( الذابل) ، فإنه لو لم يكن حياً لفسد بالتعفن والتفرق ( مع عدم قوة التغذية) فيه (و) أيضاً (في النبات قوة التغذية مع عدم الحياة ) فيه فقد وجد كل واحدة من الحياة وقوة التغذية بدون الأخرى فكانتا متغايرتين قطعاً. ومن هاهنا تبين أن أجناس القوى الموجودة في الحيوانات ثلاثة : جنس القوى النفسانية وجنس القوى الطبيعية، وجنس القوى الشروط والموانع فقائم في القوى النفسانية والطبيعية أيضاً. ولذا قال بعض شارحي القانون : اعلم أنَّ العضو الخدر إما فاقد الذات الحس والحركة، وإما لكمالهما وإما لقوتهما. وكيف كان يصح الاستدلال على التغاير بين القوة الحيوانية والقوة النفسانية وما قيل : أنَّ هذا التنوير إنما يدل على مغايرتها للقوة اللامسة والمقصود بيان مغايرتها لجميع القوى النفسانية، فمدفوع بأن مغايرتها لباقي القوى النفسانية ظاهرة لفقدانها مع بقاء الحياة . قوله : ( في العضو الذابل إلخ قيل : إِنَّ في العضو الذابل قوة التغذية، موجودة إلا أنَّ المتحلل أكثر مما يخلفه . قوله : (جنس القوى النفسانية وهي الحواس العشرة والقوى المحركة التي معدنها الدماغ ) وجنس القوى الطبيعية، وهي قوة التغذية والتنمية التي معدنها الكبد وقوة توليد المثل التي قوله : (إذ هي (الحافظة قيل عليه : إن الحافظ يجوز أن يكون المزاج الخاص أو تعلق النفس بالبدن . أجيب : بأن الكلام فيما يحفظ المزاج الخاص الذي به قوام الحياة في الحيوان الناطق ،وغيره وفيه نظر لأنهم لا يعنون بالنفس الجوهر المجرد بل مبدأ الأفاعيل والحركات المختلفة أو مبدأ الإدراك والتحريك الإرادي. قوله : ( في العضو الذابل يمكن أن يقال : توجد الغاذية مع التغذية في العضو الذابل لكن قوة التحليل أقوى فلهذا لم يظهر التغذية وقد يستدل على المغايرة بوجود قوة الحياة في الفلك عندهم مع انتفاء قوة التغذية والتنمية فيه وفيه أن المتوهم كون حياة الحيوان نفس قوة التغذية وهذا الدليل لا يبطله لجواز أن تكون حياة الفلك مخالفة بالنوع لحياة الحيوان كما هو قوله : ( جنس القوة النفسانية إلخ ) القوى النفسية هي المدركة والمحركة كما صرح به والنسبة إما إلى النفس الحيوانية أو إلى النفس الناطقة، لكونها في الإنسان أكمل منها في سائر الظاهر.
As for its difference from the natural powers that manage nutrition, his saying indicates it: 'And it (i.e., life) exists in the withered limb', for if it were not alive it would decay by putrefaction and disintegration, despite the absence of nutritive power in it. And also in plants there is nutritive power without life in them. So each of life and nutritive power has been found without the other, so they are definitely different. And from here it becomes clear that the genera of powers existing in animals are three: the genus of psychical powers, the genus of natural powers, and the genus of... conditions and obstacles exist also in psychical and natural powers. Therefore some commentators of the Canon said: Know that the numb limb is either devoid of the essence of sensation and movement, or because of their perfection, or because of their power. However, the inference of the difference between the animal power and the psychical power is valid. And what is said that this proof only indicates its difference from the sense of touch while the aim is to show its difference from all psychical powers is refuted because its difference from the rest of the psychical powers is apparent by their absence while life remains. His saying: 'In the withered limb etc.' It is said: In the withered limb the nutritive power exists, but what is decomposed is more than what is replaced. His saying: 'The genus of psychical powers: they are the ten senses and the motive powers whose mine is the brain; and the genus of natural powers: they are the power of nutrition and growth whose mine is the liver, and the power of generating the like which...' His saying: 'Since it (i.e., life) is the preserver', it is objected: the preserver could be the specific temperament or the soul's attachment to the body. The answer: the discussion concerns what preserves the specific temperament by which life subsists in the rational animal and others; but this is questionable because they do not mean by the soul the abstract substance but rather the principle of actions and various movements or the principle of perception and voluntary movement. His saying: 'In the withered limb', it can be said: the nutritive power exists with nutrition in the withered limb, but the dissolving power is stronger, so nutrition does not appear. And one may infer the difference by the presence of life in the celestial sphere according to them despite the absence of nutritive and growth powers in it; but this is countered: he who imagines that animal life is the same as nutritive power is not refuted by this argument because it is possible that the life of the celestial sphere differs in species from animal life, as is apparent. His saying: 'The genus of psychical powers etc.' The psychical powers are the perceptive and motive as stated, and the relation is either to the animal soul or to the rational soul, because in humans they are more perfect than in others.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a proof that life is distinct from the natural powers (especially nutrition) and then provides a classification of powers. The commentator first argues: a withered limb retains life (it does not putrefy) but lacks nutritive power, while plants have nutritive power without life—thus life and nutrition are distinct. From this he concludes that the genera of powers in animals are three: psychical, natural, and a third (the end is cut off). The passage then records objections and answers: for example, some object that the preservation of the withered limb could be due to temperament or the soul's attachment, but the commentator clarifies that the discussion concerns what preserves the specific temperament. Another objection is that the withered limb might still have nutrition but it is outweighed by decomposition. The commentator also considers an argument from celestial spheres (they have life but no nutrition) but notes that the objector assumes a specific type of life. The classification is then given: psychical powers include the ten senses and motor powers, natural powers include nutrition, growth, and reproduction. The psychical powers relate either to the animal or rational soul.

عضو ذابل

Correction: They are distinct because a withered limb has life without nutrition, and plants have nutrition without life.

?

?

Proving Life is Distinct from Psychical Powers: The Paralyzed Limb
Sanusi proves that life is distinct from psychical powers by the example of the paralyzed limb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of a paralyzed limb, which remains alive yet lacks sensation and movement, demonstrates that life is a distinct attribute from psychical powers.

Core Idea
A limb can be alive yet insensible—showing life is not the same as sensation or movement.

Sanusi presents a simple but powerful empirical argument: if life were identical to the powers of perception and movement, then losing those powers would mean losing life. But a paralyzed or numbed limb remains alive (it does not decay) while lacking sensation. This proves that life is a separate attribute. Let's examine the proof closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice the contrast between the dead limb and the paralyzed limb.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage mentions two examples: the paralyzed limb (al-mafluj) and the numbed limb (al-khadir). What do they have in common?
Both lack sensation and movement but remain alive.
3
Understand the contrast with a dead limb
A dead limb decays quickly, whereas the paralyzed limb does not because life preserves it. This shows life is present even without psychical powers.
4
Extract the logical conclusion
Since life can exist without sensation and movement, life must be distinct from the psychical powers (perception and movement).
The conclusion is explicitly stated at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول - المقصد الأول : تعريف الحياة
ألا ترى
المذكور ( أنها ) أي الحياة ( توجد للمفلوج من الأعضاء (إذ هي الحافظة) في الحيوان ( للأجزاء ) العنصرية المتداعية إلى الانفكاك ( عن ( التعفن و ( التفرق والبلى ) أنَّ العضو الميت تتسارع إليه هذه الأمور، وليس له ) أي للعضو المفلوج (قوة الحس والحركة) وكذا الحال في العضو الخدر، فإنه أيضاً فاقد في الحال قوة الحس والحركة، مع وجود قوة الحياة فيه. فظهر أنَّ الحياة مغايرة للقوى النفسانية التي هي القوى المدركة والمحركة.
The first type - first objective: Definition of life. Do you not see that it is mentioned that life exists in the paralyzed limb (since it is the preserver) in the animal of the elemental parts prone to dissolution from decay, disintegration, and wear, that the dead limb quickly succumbs to these things, while the paralyzed limb has no power of sensation and movement. Similarly the numbed limb, for it also currently lacks the power of sensation and movement, while the power of life exists in it. So it appears that life is different from the psychical powers, which are the powers of perception and movement.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a proof by example. The author argues that life (al-hayat) is not identical to the psychical powers (al-quwa al-nafsaniyya) of sensation and movement. The evidence is that a paralyzed limb (al-mafluj) or a numbed limb (al-khadir) still retains life—it does not decay—yet it lacks sensation and voluntary movement. Therefore, life must be a distinct entity from these powers. The author follows a simple argument: if life were the same as sensation and movement, then when sensation and movement are lost, life should also be lost. But we observe the opposite: the limb remains alive (it does not putrefy) while sensation and movement are absent. Hence, they are different.

Key Terms
الحياة
القوى النفسانية
المفلوج
الخدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that life is a separate substance or entity that can exist independently.
Correction: The argument only shows conceptual distinction, not that life can exist apart from the body entirely. Life still inheres in the body.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence used to show that life is different from psychical powers?
A) The dead limb decays quickly
B) The paralyzed limb has no sensation but remains alive
C) Plants have nutrition without life
D) The heart is the seat of life

Key Takeaway
Life is a distinct attribute from psychical powers, as shown by the living but insensible limb.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a limb can be alive yet insensible, what does that say about your own moments of spiritual numbness? Your faith may still be alive, but where have you lost the 'movement' of worship or the 'sensation' of gratitude? Will you acknowledge that area and ask God to restore those psychical powers without doubting the life He has already given you?
?
The paralyzed limb does not decay; it is preserved by life despite its inability. What part of your soul is still preserved by God's grace even though you feel it is paralyzed—unable to act or perceive as you wish? Can you trust that your worth before God is rooted in His gift of life, not in your performance? What one step can you take today to act despite the numbness?

The Withered Limb and Plants: Proving Life Distinct from Natural Powers
Sanusi proves life's distinction from natural powers using withered limb and plants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the withered limb and plants demonstrate that life is distinct from the natural power of nutrition.

Core Idea
Life and nutrition are independent—each can exist without the other.

Sanusi presents a second proof that life is not identical to natural powers. He uses two examples: a withered limb that has life but lacks nutrition, and plants that have nutrition but lack animal life. This shows they are separate. Read the passage to see the logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the two examples given.
The passage is concise; each example is a short phrase.
2
Identify the two examples
Find the withered limb example and the plant example. Notice how each shows one attribute without the other.
The withered limb has life but no nutrition; plants have nutrition but no animal life.
3
Understand the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that life and nutrition are definitely distinct (متغايرتين قطعاً).
This is a logical proof based on separation of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مغايرتها للقوى الطبيعية التي تتصرف في الأغذية فيدل عليها قوله : ( وتوجد ) أي الحياة (في) العضو ( الذابل) ، فإنه لو لم يكن حياً لفسد بالتعفن والتفرق ( مع عدم قوة التغذية) فيه (و) أيضاً (في النبات قوة التغذية مع عدم الحياة ) فيه فقد وجد كل واحدة من الحياة وقوة التغذية بدون الأخرى فكانتا متغايرتين قطعاً. ومن هاهنا تبين أن أجناس القوى الموجودة في الحيوانات ثلاثة : جنس القوى النفسانية وجنس القوى الطبيعية، وجنس القوى الحيوانية.
As for its distinction from the natural powers that manage nutrition, it is indicated by his statement: 'And it (i.e., life) exists in the withered limb', for if it were not alive, it would decay and disintegrate, while it lacks the power of nutrition. Also, in plants there is the power of nutrition without life in it. So each of life and the power of nutrition has been found without the other, so they are definitely distinct. From here it becomes clear that the genera of powers present in animals are three: the genus of psychical powers, the genus of natural powers, and the genus of animal powers.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a second proof for the distinction of life from natural powers (quwa tabi'iyya), specifically the power of nutrition (quwwat al-taghdiya). The evidence is twofold: (1) In a withered limb (al-dhabil), life is present (the limb does not decay) yet it lacks the power of nutrition. (2) In plants, there is the power of nutrition but no life (as plants are not considered to have animal life). Thus, each can exist without the other, proving they are distinct. The author then draws a conclusion: there are three genera of powers in animals: psychical (perception and movement), natural (nutrition, growth, reproduction), and animal (life itself, whose seat is the heart). This classification is important for understanding the composition of living beings.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Plants have no life at all.
Correction: Plants lack animal life but have their own form of life (vegetative life). The argument only separates animal life from nutrition.

D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Life and nutrition are distinct attributes; their separate existence proves they are not identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change if you thanked Him for that life rather than lamenting the lack of nutrition? This week, take 5 minutes to reflect on one such area and praise Allah for the life He has given there.
?
Plants receive nutrition but have no animal life—they exist only to consume and grow. Is there any part of your spiritual life where you are merely going through the motions (receiving ‘nutrition’ from Islamic knowledge) without true life (ikhlas, love, fear)? Identify one act of worship you do out of habit and commit to rekindling the life in it before your next lesson.

The Three Genera of Powers in Animals
Sanusi enumerates three genera of powers in animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three genera of powers present in animals as derived from the distinction of life.

Core Idea
Animals possess three distinct genera of powers: psychical, natural, and animal.

Having established that life is distinct from natural powers, Sanusi now generalizes to all powers in animals. He identifies three genera: psychical (related to perception and movement), natural (nutrition, growth, reproduction), and animal (life itself). This classification is fundamental for understanding animal faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that it follows directly from the previous proof.
The word 'من هاهنا' shows it is a conclusion from the earlier argument.
2
List the three genera
Identify each genus: psychical powers, natural powers, and animal powers.
These are the three categories mentioned.
3
Reflect on the classification
Think about how these genera are distinct and what each includes. Psychical powers are for perception and movement, natural for growth and nutrition, animal for life itself.

Refuting Ibn Sina's Objection on Hindrances
Sanusi responds to Ibn Sina's objection about hindrances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the proof from paralyzed limbs against Ibn Sina's objection about hindrances, clarifying that absence of act does not prove absence of power.

Core Idea
A hindrance can block action without removing power.

Ibn Sina objected that the absence of sensation in a paralyzed limb might be due to a hindrance, not lack of power. Sanusi responds by conceding the possibility but arguing that this does not undermine the proof, because the proof relies on the distinction between life and those powers, not on the absence of the powers themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi structures his response: first addressing the paralyzed limb, then the withered limb, then rejecting the plant analogy.
Pay attention to the term 'mani'' (hindrance) – it is central to the argument.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's objection
Ibn Sina claims that the absence of action (sensation, movement, nutrition) does not prove the absence of power because a hindrance could be present. Identify this claim in the passage.
3
Understand Sanusi's response
Sanusi concedes the possibility of a hindrance but argues that the original proof does not require the power to be absent. The proof only shows that life is distinct from these powers. Trace how Sanusi maintains his position despite the objection.
The key is that the proof from the paralyzed limb is about the coexistence of life with absence of act, not about absence of power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكره ابن سينا : (إِنّا لا نسلم أن القوة) أي أن قوة الحس والحركة (مفقودة في ) العضو ( المفلوج و أن قوة التغذية مفقودة في العضو ( الذابل لجواز أن يكون الفعل ) أي الإحساس والحركة والتغذية قد تخلف عنها أي عن القوة الموجودة فيهما (لمانع) يمنعها عن فعلها . والحاصل أن المفقود في العضو المفلوج هو الفعل، أعني الإحساس والحركة الإرادية، وذلك لا يدل على أن القوة المقتضية لهما مفقودة فيه، لجواز أن يكون عدم الفعل لوجود المانع لا لعدم المقتضي، وكذلك المفقودة في العضو الذابل هو التغذية وليس يلزم من فقدانها فقدان القوة المقتضية لها ولا نسلم أيضاً أنَّ ما هو قوة التغذية في الحي موجود في النبات ) حتى يلزم من مغايرة الحياة لغاذية النبات مغايرتها لغاذية الحيوان، وذلك ) لجواز أن تكون قوة التغذية في معدتها الاثنين وجنس القوى الحيوانية التي معدنها القلب وهي قوة الحياة وإطلاق الجنس عليها إما للازدواج أو لاختلاف أنواعها بحسب اختلاف أنواع الحيوان .
And the answer to what Ibn Sina mentioned: 'We do not concede that the power (i.e., the power of sensation and movement) is absent in the paralyzed limb, nor that the power of nutrition is absent in the withered limb, because it is possible that the act (i.e., sensation, movement, and nutrition) may be absent from the existing power due to a hindrance that prevents its action. The bottom line is that what is missing in the paralyzed limb is the act, namely sensation and voluntary movement, and that does not indicate that the power that necessitates them is absent, because it is possible that the absence of the act is due to the existence of a hindrance, not the absence of the efficient cause. Similarly, what is missing in the withered limb is nutrition, and its absence does not necessitate the absence of the power that necessitates it. And we also do not concede that what is the power of nutrition in animals exists in plants, such that the distinction of life from plant nutrition necessitates its distinction from animal nutrition, because it is possible that the power of nutrition in... (the text becomes unclear) and the genus of animal powers whose seat is the heart and which is the power of life, and calling it a genus is either for pairing or due to the variation of its types according to the variation of animal types.'
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. Ibn Sina argued that the proof from the paralyzed and withered limbs is invalid because the absence of sensation or nutrition could be due to a hindrance (mani'), not because the power itself is absent. The author (Sanusi) responds by conceding the possibility but arguing that the original proof does not rely on the absence of the power; rather, it relies on the presence of life alongside the absence of the act. The act (fi'l) is missing, but the power (quwwa) might still be present. However, the author maintains that the absence of the act does not prove the absence of the power, so the argument that life is distinct from those powers is not undermined. He further rejects the analogy with plants, saying that the power of nutrition in plants may be different in kind from that in animals. The passage ends by reaffirming the three genera of powers.

Misconception: If an action is absent, then the power that produces it must also be absent.
Correction: Absence of action can be due to a hindrance (mani'), not necessarily absence of power. The proof from the paralyzed limb does not require the power to be absent.

Life Requires a Specific Bodily Structure
Lists the conditions for life according to philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to the philosophers, life is not an independent attribute but depends on a specific bodily structure, form, and balanced temperament.

Core Idea
Life is not standalone; it depends on a specific physical structure.

The passage begins by stating the philosophers' condition for life: it is conditioned by a specific structure. Before we can discuss life as an attribute, we must understand its physical prerequisites. This lesson lays out the basic requirements as agreed upon by the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author lists the components required for life.
The passage uses a compressed style typical of philosophical texts.
2
Identify the components required for life
Look for the phrases that enumerate what life depends on: a body composed of elements, a specific form, qualities (including temperament), and the animal spirit.
The mention of 'specific form' and 'qualities that follow it' is key.
3
Understand the phrase 'qualities that follow it'
The commentator clarifies that these qualities, including life itself, are consequent upon the specific form. This shows the hierarchical dependence.
This phrase is often misunderstood as redundant.
4
Reflect on the philosophical significance
Ask yourself: Why do philosophers insist on a physical substrate for life? How does this view contrast with other theories?
This connects to the learning objective about life not being independent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول - المقصد الثاني : الحياة مشروطة بالبنية المخصوصة في شرط الحياة ( الحياة عند الحكماء مشروطة بالبنية المخصوصة وهو جسم مركب من العناصر له صورة نوعية مخصوصة ولذلك الجسم كيفيات تتبعها أي تتبع هذه الكيفيات تلك الصورة المخصوصة من اعتدال مزاجي خاص وغيره . فإنهم زعموا أنه لا بد في الحياة من جسم مؤلف من العناصر الأربعة، ومن مزاج معتدل مناسب لنوع من الحيوانات حتى يفيض عليه صورة نوعية حيوانية مستتبعة للحياة، ولا بد فيها من اعتدال الروح الحيواني المتولد من بخارية الأخلاط. قوله : ( أي تتبع هذه الكيفيات التي من جملتها الحياة فتكون مشروطة بالصورة النوعية المشروطة بالنية المخصوصة وبهذا ظهر فائدة قوله : ولذلك الجسم كيفيات تتبعها، وأن تفسيره بأنه تتبع هذه الكيفيات تلك الصورة النوعية المخصوصة لغو من الكلام لا دخل له في المقصود على أنه ليس لها سوى الاعتدال النوعي كيفية تتبع الصورة النوعية إياها فكيف يصح قوله : من اعتدال خاص وغيره . قوله : ( من اعتدال مزاجي خص الاعتدال بالمزاجي لأن الاعتدال الروحي ليس لذلك الجسم المركب من العناصر بل للروح الحاصل من الأخلاط . قوله : ( فإنهم زعموا إلخ يريد أنّ الحياة مشروطة بالنسبة لوجهين. أحدهما : من حيث الفاعل فإن الحياة تابعة للصورة النوعية المقتضية لها التابعة للاعتدال المزاجي الذي لا يحصل إلا بالبنية المخصوصة وثانيهما باعتبار الحامل فإنَّ الحياة لا تفيض إلا على الروح الحيواني من لطافة الأخلاط التي لا تحصل إلا بالبنية قوله : (المتولدة من بخارية الأخلاط إلخ) أي من صيرورة الاخلاط بخاراً فإنه، جسم لطيف حتى قوله: (أي تتبع هذه الكيفيات تلك الصورة (المخصوصة) المناسب لقوله الآتي يفيض عليه صورة نوعية أن يجعل هذه الكيفيات مفعول تتبع وتلك الصورة فاعلها، لكن الكلام في وجود كيفية غير الاعتدال متبوعة للصورة كما يدل عليه الكيفيات أيضاً، ويمكن أن يعكس حديث الفاعلية والمفعولية أن يراد بالتبعية التبعية باعتبار البقاء كما سيحققه فليتأمل. قوله : (اعتدال الروح الحيواني الروح الحيواني جسم لطيف بخاري يتكون من الأخلاط ينبعث من التجويف الأيسر من القلب ويسري إلى البدن في عروق ثابتة من القلب تسمى بالشرايين. الحامل لقوة الحياة إلى أعضاء البدن على ما فصل في الكتب الطبية ثم إن بقاء المزاج والروح الحيواني على اعتدالهما المعتبر في بقاء الحياة تابع لتلك الصورة النوعية. فإذا تغير المزاج وزال عن الاعتدال بسبب من الأسباب زالت الحياة وانتقضت البنية واضمحلت الصورة كما يشاهد ذلك في الحيوانات بمساعدة التجربة
The second objective: Life is conditioned by a specific structure. On the condition of life: Life according to the philosophers is conditioned by a specific structure, which is a body composed of elements, having a specific specific form, and for that body are qualities that follow it (i.e., these qualities follow that specific form) of a particular temperament and other things. For they claimed that life must have a body composed of the four elements, and a balanced temperament suitable for a type of animal, so that a specific living form that entails life may emanate upon it, and there must be a balance of the animal spirit generated from the vaporous humors. The commentator says: 'i.e., these qualities, among which is life, follow the specific form conditioned by the specific structure.' ... The commentator says: 'He specified temperament as mixed because spiritual balance is not for that body composed of elements but for the spirit resulting from humors.' ... The commentator says: 'He means that life is conditioned in two respects: one from the agent, for life depends on the specific form that necessitates it, which depends on the temperamental balance that only occurs with the specific structure; and the second from the carrier, for life only emanates upon the animal spirit from the subtlety of humors that only occurs with the structure.' ... The commentator says: 'The animal spirit is a subtle vaporous body formed from the humors, emanating from the left cavity of the heart and flowing to the body through fixed veins from the heart called arteries. It carries the power of life to the organs of the body as detailed in medical books. Then the persistence of the temperament and the animal spirit in their balanced state required for the persistence of life depends on that specific form. So when the temperament changes and leaves balance due to some cause, life ceases, the structure dissolves, and the form disappears, as observed in animals by experience.'
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage explains the philosophers' position that life is conditioned by a specific bodily structure. The commentator begins by quoting the original statement that life requires a body composed of elements, a specific form, and qualities including a particular temperament. He then clarifies that the phrase 'qualities that follow it' means these qualities (including life) follow the specific form. He highlights that the original author specified 'temperamental' balance because spiritual balance belongs to the spirit, not the elemental body. He then explains two aspects of conditionality: from the agent side, life depends on the specific form which depends on temperamental balance requiring the specific structure; from the carrier side, life only emanates on the animal spirit which requires the specific structure. He then defines the animal spirit as a subtle vaporous body emanating from the heart and spreading through arteries. Finally, he explains that the persistence of life depends on the persistence of the balanced temperament and spirit, which are maintained by the specific form. When the temperament changes due to internal or external causes, life ceases, the structure dissolves, and the form disappears, as empirically observed.

البنية المخصوصة
الحكماء
الصورة النوعية
المزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That philosophers consider life independent of any physical substrate; they think life can exist without a body.
Correction: The passage clearly states that life is conditioned by a specific bodily structure, form, temperament, and spirit. Life cannot exist apart from these physical prerequisites.

Life, according to philosophers, is not an independent attribute but depends on a specific bodily structure, form, balanced temperament, and animal spirit.

?

The Two Respects of Conditionality: Agent and Carrier
Explains the two respects of conditionality for life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that life is conditioned in two respects: from the agent side (the specific form) and from the carrier side (the animal spirit), both ultimately requiring the specific bodily structure.

Core Idea
Life is conditioned both by its cause (form) and its vessel (spirit).

The commentator elaborates on the philosophers' view by distinguishing two aspects of conditionality. Understanding these two respects clarifies why the specific structure is indispensable: it provides both the formal cause and the material substrate for life.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary on the two respects
Locate the passage where the commentator explains that life is conditioned in two ways: from the agent and from the carrier.
The commentator uses 'fāʿil' (agent) and 'ḥāmil' (carrier).
2
Understand the agent respect
The agent respect means that life depends on the specific form, which depends on temperamental balance, which in turn requires the specific structure. Trace this chain.
Here, the form is the efficient cause of life.
3
Understand the carrier respect
The carrier respect means that life only emanates upon the animal spirit, which requires subtle humors, which require the specific structure. Trace this chain.
The spirit is the substrate that receives life.
4
Synthesize both respects
Realize that both respects converge on the necessity of the specific bodily structure. The structure is needed for both the formal cause and the material carrier.
This shows the unity of the philosophical position.

Source Text

النوع الأول - المقصد الثاني : الحياة مشروطة بالبنية المخصوصة في شرط الحياة ( الحياة عند الحكماء مشروطة بالبنية المخصوصة وهو جسم مركب من العناصر له صورة نوعية مخصوصة ولذلك الجسم كيفيات تتبعها أي تتبع هذه الكيفيات تلك الصورة المخصوصة من اعتدال مزاجي خاص وغيره . فإنهم زعموا أنه لا بد في الحياة من جسم مؤلف من العناصر الأربعة، ومن مزاج معتدل مناسب لنوع من الحيوانات حتى يفيض عليه صورة نوعية حيوانية مستتبعة للحياة، ولا بد فيها من اعتدال الروح الحيواني المتولد من بخارية الأخلاط. قوله : ( أي تتبع هذه الكيفيات التي من جملتها الحياة فتكون مشروطة بالصورة النوعية المشروطة بالنية المخصوصة وبهذا ظهر فائدة قوله : ولذلك الجسم كيفيات تتبعها، وأن تفسيره بأنه تتبع هذه الكيفيات تلك الصورة النوعية المخصوصة لغو من الكلام لا دخل له في المقصود على أنه ليس لها سوى الاعتدال النوعي كيفية تتبع الصورة النوعية إياها فكيف يصح قوله : من اعتدال خاص وغيره . قوله : ( من اعتدال مزاجي خص الاعتدال بالمزاجي لأن الاعتدال الروحي ليس لذلك الجسم المركب من العناصر بل للروح الحاصل من الأخلاط . قوله : ( فإنهم زعموا إلخ يريد أنّ الحياة مشروطة بالنسبة لوجهين. أحدهما : من حيث الفاعل فإن الحياة تابعة للصورة النوعية المقتضية لها التابعة للاعتدال المزاجي الذي لا يحصل إلا بالبنية المخصوصة وثانيهما باعتبار الحامل فإنَّ الحياة لا تفيض إلا على الروح الحيواني من لطافة الأخلاط التي لا تحصل إلا بالبنية قوله : (المتولدة من بخارية الأخلاط إلخ) أي من صيرورة الاخلاط بخاراً فإنه، جسم لطيف حتى قوله: (أي تتبع هذه الكيفيات تلك الصورة (المخصوصة) المناسب لقوله الآتي يفيض عليه صورة نوعية أن يجعل هذه الكيفيات مفعول تتبع وتلك الصورة فاعلها، لكن الكلام في وجود كيفية غير الاعتدال متبوعة للصورة كما يدل عليه الكيفيات أيضاً، ويمكن أن يعكس حديث الفاعلية والمفعولية أن يراد بالتبعية التبعية باعتبار البقاء كما سيحققه فليتأمل. قوله : (اعتدال الروح الحيواني الروح الحيواني جسم لطيف بخاري يتكون من الأخلاط ينبعث من التجويف الأيسر من القلب ويسري إلى البدن في عروق ثابتة من القلب تسمى بالشرايين. الحامل لقوة الحياة إلى أعضاء البدن على ما فصل في الكتب الطبية ثم إن بقاء المزاج والروح الحيواني على اعتدالهما المعتبر في بقاء الحياة تابع لتلك الصورة النوعية. فإذا تغير المزاج وزال عن الاعتدال بسبب من الأسباب زالت الحياة وانتقضت البنية واضمحلت الصورة كما يشاهد ذلك في الحيوانات بمساعدة التجربة
The second objective: Life is conditioned by a specific structure. On the condition of life: Life according to the philosophers is conditioned by a specific structure, which is a body composed of elements, having a specific specific form, and for that body are qualities that follow it (i.e., these qualities follow that specific form) of a particular temperament and other things. For they claimed that life must have a body composed of the four elements, and a balanced temperament suitable for a type of animal, so that a specific living form that entails life may emanate upon it, and there must be a balance of the animal spirit generated from the vaporous humors. The commentator says: 'i.e., these qualities, among which is life, follow the specific form conditioned by the specific structure.' ... The commentator says: 'He specified temperament as mixed because spiritual balance is not for that body composed of elements but for the spirit resulting from humors.' ... The commentator says: 'He means that life is conditioned in two respects: one from the agent, for life depends on the specific form that necessitates it, which depends on the temperamental balance that only occurs with the specific structure; and the second from the carrier, for life only emanates upon the animal spirit from the subtlety of humors that only occurs with the structure.' ... The commentator says: 'The animal spirit is a subtle vaporous body formed from the humors, emanating from the left cavity of the heart and flowing to the body through fixed veins from the heart called arteries. It carries the power of life to the organs of the body as detailed in medical books. Then the persistence of the temperament and the animal spirit in their balanced state required for the persistence of life depends on that specific form. So when the temperament changes and leaves balance due to some cause, life ceases, the structure dissolves, and the form disappears, as observed in animals by experience.'
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage explains the philosophers' position that life is conditioned by a specific bodily structure. The commentator begins by quoting the original statement that life requires a body composed of elements, a specific form, and qualities including a particular temperament. He then clarifies that the phrase 'qualities that follow it' means these qualities (including life) follow the specific form. He highlights that the original author specified 'temperamental' balance because spiritual balance belongs to the spirit, not the elemental body. He then explains two aspects of conditionality: from the agent side, life depends on the specific form which depends on temperamental balance requiring the specific structure; from the carrier side, life only emanates on the animal spirit which requires the specific structure. He then defines the animal spirit as a subtle vaporous body emanating from the heart and spreading through arteries. Finally, he explains that the persistence of life depends on the persistence of the balanced temperament and spirit, which are maintained by the specific form. When the temperament changes due to internal or external causes, life ceases, the structure dissolves, and the form disappears, as empirically observed.

Key Terms
الفاعل
الحامل
الصورة النوعية
الاعتدال المزاجي
الروح الحيواني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That life is conditioned in only one way, either by the form or by the spirit alone.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that life is conditioned in two respects: from the agent and from the carrier. Both are necessary and both require the specific structure.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, from which two respects is life conditioned?
A) From the material and the efficient cause
B) From the agent and the carrier
C) From the form and the matter
D) From the soul and the body

Key Takeaway
Life is conditioned both from the agent side (the form) and from the carrier side (the spirit), and both depend on the specific bodily structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very life, as this lesson shows, depends on both a cause and a carrier—neither of which you created. How does it feel to realize that you are not self-sufficient? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived with that awareness?
?
If life requires a specific bodily structure and a balanced temperament, what does that say about your responsibility to maintain your health and humors? Are you treating your body as a trust from the One who structured it, or as your own possession?
?
The passage teaches that life emanates upon the animal spirit from subtle humors. What are the 'humors' of your soul—your thoughts, intentions, and attachments? Are they subtle enough to receive the life of faith, or are they clouded with worldliness? What will you clear today?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
The Animal Spirit and the Persistence of Life
Defines animal spirit and explains the persistence of life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the animal spirit and how the persistence of life depends on the persistence of balanced temperament and spirit, which are maintained by the specific form.

Core Idea
Life persists as long as the form maintains balance; any imbalance brings death.

The passage concludes with a detailed description of the animal spirit and an empirical observation. It clarifies that life is not permanent but contingent on the continued balance of the temperament and spirit, both sustained by the specific form. This lesson connects the philosophical theory to observable reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of animal spirit
Find the sentence that defines the animal spirit: a subtle vaporous body formed from humors, emanating from the heart and flowing through arteries.
This definition is based on ancient medical theory.
2
Understand the function of the animal spirit
The spirit carries the power of life to the organs. It is the carrier of life.
3
Comprehend the persistence condition
The passage states that the persistence of life depends on the persistence of the balanced temperament and animal spirit, which are maintained by the specific form.
This shows that life is not self-subsistent.
4
Consider the empirical example
When the temperament changes due to internal or external causes, life ceases, structure dissolves, and form disappears. This is observed in animals by experience.
This bridges philosophy and empirical observation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That life is permanent and does not depend on continuous physical balance; once alive, always alive regardless of changes.
Correction: The passage explicitly says that life ceases when the temperament leaves its balanced state. Life is contingent on continued balance.

Checkpoint
What happens when the temperament changes and leaves balance according to the passage?
A) Life continues but the spirit changes
B) Life ceases, structure dissolves, form disappears
C) The specific form becomes stronger
D) The animal spirit becomes more subtle

Key Takeaway
Life persists only as long as the specific form maintains the balanced temperament and animal spirit; any imbalance leads to death, as observed empirically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life depends on a subtle balance you cannot see or control. When was the last time you genuinely felt the fragility of your existence and turned to the One who holds that balance? What would it take for you to live today with that awareness?
?
The commentator says life persists only when the specific form maintains balance. That form is a gift from the Creator. Are you living as though your life is a loan that can be recalled at any moment—or as if you own it permanently? How does your daily schedule reflect the answer?
?
If every heartbeat depends on a balanced temperament that can be undone by a single change, what does that say about your pretensions to self-sufficiency? Will you let this lesson transform your pride into broken gratitude before Allah?
